Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Trees, Crops, and Fruits in the Sale of Land
Covers inclusion of trees, crops, fruits and the dispute on immediate cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which agricultural items (trees, crops, fruits) are automatically included in the sale of land and which require explicit mention, and evaluate the cross-madhhab dispute on immediate cutting.

Core Idea
Trees enter the sale without mention; crops and fruits do not, because they are connected for separation, not stability.

When you buy farmland, do the trees and crops automatically belong to you? The Hanafi school distinguishes between items attached for stability (trees) and those attached for eventual separation (crops and fruits). This lesson explains that rule, along with the important exception for underground crops, and covers a major dispute with the other schools about whether the seller must immediately cut the crops after sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of the vegetation
First determine whether the plant is a tree (shajar) or a crop (zirāʻ) / fruit (thamr). Trees are perennial and stable; crops and fruits are seasonal and harvested.
Dry trees are considered firewood and are not automatically included.
2
Apply the ittisal qarar test
Trees are connected to the land for stability (ittisal qarar), so they enter. Crops and fruits are connected for eventual separation (ittisal li-l-fasl), so they do not enter without explicit mention or condition.
Underground crops like leeks that stay for years are treated like trees.
3
Analyze the dispute on immediate cutting
After the sale, the Hanafi school says the buyer can force the seller to cut and remove crops/fruits immediately because delivery (taslim) is an obligation of the contract. The other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) say the seller may leave them until ripe/harvest, based on customary delivery.
The Hanafi response distinguishes sale from lease: in lease, the tenant may leave crops for an equivalent rent because the land is for usufruct.
4
Consider the hadith on fruit
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever buys land with palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise.' This hadith applies equally to pollinated and unpollinated fruit in the Hanafi school.
Other schools differentiate based on pollination (ta'bir).

(ط، مستخلص) مي في بيع الدار الأن في العرف يطلق اسم الدار على العرصة والبناء (عيني) أي فيما إذا كانت المفاتيح والأغلاق من الخشب أي المتصل بالباب وإذا كانت من الحديد غير متصل به لا يدخل بالإجماع، والحاصل أن المتصل بالدار يكون تبعاً لها، ويدخل في البيع كالسلم المتصل وحجر الرحى الأسفل المبنية في الدار والبئر وبكرتها، وكذا البستان الذي فيها ولو كبيراً، فإذا كان الغلق متصلاً بالباب ومركباً فيها مثل الكيلون والضبة، وهما الذهب العريض أو الفضة العريضة يجعل على وجه الباب، فتدخل المفاتيح لم يكن الغلق مركباً فيها كالقفل لا يدخل؛ لعدم الاتصال، ولا المفاتيح، وفي القياس لا تدخل المفاتيح؛ لأنها تبعاً، وإن ليس من مسمى المبيع، ولا هي وجه الاستحسان: أن المفاتيح تبع للأغلاق؛ إذ لا ينتفع بكل واحد منهما بدون الآخر، ثم اعلم أن ما يدخل في البيع تبعاً لم يكن له حصة من الثمن إلا بالتسمية، ودخول البناء في بيع الدار؛ لقوله عليه: "من ملك أرضاً ملك ما فوقها إلى السماء، وما تحتها من الثرى". (عيني، معدن، فتح)  كتاب البيوع ٢٥٧ ما يدخل في البيع والشجر في بيع الأرض بلا ذكر، ولا يدخل الزرع في بيع الأرض بلا تسمية أي ويدخل الشجر ولا الثمر في بيع الشجر إلا بالشرط، ويقال للبائع: أي ولا يدخل الثمر في الصورتين والشجر إلخ: أي تبعاً مطلقاً ولو صغيراً أو غير مثمر، إلا أن يكون يابساً؛ لأنها بعد اليبس حطب لا شجر، وقوله: "بلا ذكر" يتعلق بقوله: "يدخل" في المسألتين، أي بلا ذكر هذه الأشياء في البيع، والأصل في هذا الباب أن كل شيء متصل بالمبيع اتصال قرار يدخل في البيع، وإلا فلا إلا إذا جرى العرف بالدخول فيه كالمفاتيح والسلم المتصل بالبناء يدخل ولو كان من خشب، وغير المتصل لا يدخل رمز الحقائق: ۷/۲، ۸] (فتح) بلا ذكر: راجع إلى المسألتين؛ لأنه متصل بها للقرار فصار كالبناء. (عيني) الزرع إلخ: لأنه متصل بها للفصل من الآدمي، فلا يرد حمل الجارية؛ لأن ذلك بفصل الله، ولو كان الزرع مغيباً في الأرض من الكراث يدخل في البيع المطلق على الصحيح؛ لأنه يبقى سنين بمنزلة الشجر لا ما كان ظاهراً، والورد وورق التوت والآس ونحوها كالثمار. تتمة: ثياب الغلام والجارية تدخل في البيع بغير شرط للعرف إلا أن تكون ثيابا فاخرة تلبس عند العرض على البيع لا يدخل بدون شرط وخطام البعير والحبل عنق الحمار، والبرذعة والإكاف يدخل للعرف، بخلاف السرج، وولد الناقة والرمكة والأتان والبقر والمعز إن ذهب به مع الأم إلى موضع البيع دخل فيه للعرف، وإلا فلا. (عيني، فتح بلا تسمية إلا إذا ينبت ولا قيمة له فيدخل في الأصح، وكذا إذا لم ينبت بعد. (عيني) ولا الثمر إلخ لأن اتصال الزرع والثمر ليس للقرار، فصارا كالمتاع الموضوع في الدار، وعبر ههنا بـ"الشرط" وفي الزرع بـ"التسمية؛ ليفيد عدم الفرق، وأن هذا الشرط غير مفسد وخصه بالثمر اتباعا لقوله عليه: "من اشترى أرضا فيها نخل فالثمرة للبائع إلا أن يشترط المبتاع من غير فصل بين المؤبر وغيره، ويقوم مقام التسمية، والشرط ما لو قال: "بكل قليل وكثير هو فيها" إلا أن يبينه بقوله : من حقوقها أو من مرافقها؛ لأن الحق يذكر منه كالطريق والشرب والمرافق ما يرتفق به كمسيل الماء. " هو تبع لابد للمبيع . والتأبير: أن يشق وعاء نحل أنثى، فيجعل فيه شيء من طلع نخل ذكر وهو إصلاح للثمر ، وعند الثلاثة: لو لم يكن الثمرة مؤبرة تدخل، وإلا فلا وفي القياس يدخل الزرع والثمر، وكان وجه القياس بالنظر لمطلق الاتصال بالمبيع، وإن لم يكن على وجه القرار. (عيني، فتح) بالشرط: لأن اتصالهما للقرار فصار كالمتاع الموضوع في الدار. ويقال للبائع إلخ: أي بعد نقد المشتري الثمن : اقطع الزرع والثمرة، وسلم الأرض والشجرة؛ لأن ملك المشتري مشغول بملك البائع، فكان عليه تفريغه وتسليمه، كما إذا كان فيه متاع موضوع، وقال الشافعي: يترك حتى يظهر صلاح الثمرة - أي ينتفع به الدواب أو الإنسان - ويستحصد الزرع؛ لأن الواجب هو التسليم المعتاد، وفي العادة أن لا يقطع كذلك، وصار كما إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض زرع لم يدرك، فإنه يترك إلى الحصاد، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا أن التسليم واجب بحكم العقد، بخلاف الإجارة؛ لأنها للانتفاع، فيترك بأجر المثل، =
Section: On explaining what enters the sale without mention and what does not enter, and other things. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 7/2] And al-mafatih: plural of miftah, keys of locks not keys of padlocks, and what is connected to the building enters. (T, Mustakhlas) Meem-Ya: In a sale of a house now, in custom the name 'house' applies to the vacant land and the building (Ayni) meaning when the keys and locks are of wood, i.e., connected to the door, and if they are of iron not connected to it, it does not enter by consensus. The gist is that what is connected to the house is a dependent and enters the sale, like the attached stairs and the lower millstone built in the house, and the well and its pulley, and likewise the garden that is in it even if large. So if the lock is connected to the door and fixed in it like the kilon and dabbah, which are broad gold or broad silver placed on the face of the door, then [their] keys enter; if the lock is not fixed in it like a padlock, it does not enter due to lack of connection, nor the keys. And by qiyas the keys would not enter because they are not part of the named sold item, nor are they connected. The basis of istihsan is that keys are dependent on locks, since neither can be used without the other. Then know that what enters the sale as a dependent does not have a share of the price unless specified. And the entry of the building in the sale of a house is due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it to the sky and what is below it to the depths.' (Ayni, Madan, Fath) [Page 258] The Book of Sales. 257. What enters the sale. And trees [enter] in the sale of land without mention, and crops do not enter in the sale of land without specification, i.e., trees enter, and fruit does not enter in the sale of trees except by condition, and it is said to the seller: i.e., fruit does not enter in both cases. And 'trees etc.': i.e., absolutely as a dependent, even if small or non-fruit-bearing, unless it is dry, because after dryness it is firewood, not trees. And his statement 'without mention' relates to his statement 'enters' in both issues, i.e., without mention of these things in the sale. The principle in this chapter is that everything connected to the sold item with a connection of stability enters the sale, otherwise not unless custom includes it, like keys and stairs attached to the building enter even if of wood, and what is not attached does not enter. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 7/2, 8) (Fath) 'without mention': refers to both issues because it is connected to them for stability, so it becomes like the building. (Ayni) 'Crops etc.': because they are connected to them for separation by humans, so it does not apply to the pregnancy of a slave girl because that is by God's separation. And if the crop is buried in the ground like leeks, it enters in an absolute sale according to the correct view, because it remains for years like trees, unlike what is above ground. And roses, mulberry leaves, myrtle, and the like are like fruits. Completion: The clothes of a slave boy and slave girl enter the sale without condition because of custom, unless they are fine clothes worn for display at the time of sale, in which case they do not enter without condition. And the nose-ring of a camel, the rope around a donkey's neck, the packsaddle and the saddle blanket enter because of custom, unlike the saddle. And the offspring of a she-camel, a white mare, a female donkey, a cow, and a goat, if taken with the mother to the place of sale, enter because of custom, otherwise not. (Ayni, Fath) Without specification unless it is growing and has no value, then it enters according to the most correct; likewise if it has not yet grown. (Ayni) 'And not fruit etc.' because the connection of crops and fruit is not for stability, so they become like goods placed in the house. He used 'condition' here and 'specification' for crops to indicate there is no difference, and that this condition is not invalidating. He specified fruit following his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever buys land containing palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates [otherwise]', without distinguishing between pollinated and unpollinated. And it stands in place of specification; and the condition is if he says, 'With every little and much that is in it', unless he specifies by saying 'from its rights or from its appurtenances'; because rights are mentioned from it like the path and drinking place, and appurtenances are what one benefits from like the watercourse. It is a dependent inseparable from the sold item. And al-ta'bir: that one splits the vessel of a female date palm and puts some pollen from a male date palm into it, and it is improvement of fruit. According to the three [imams], if the fruit is not pollinated it enters, otherwise not. By qiyas, crops and fruit would enter, and the basis of qiyas is considering absolute connection to the sold item even if not for stability. (Ayni, Fath) 'By condition': because their connection is for stability, so they become like goods placed in the house. 'And it is said to the seller etc.': i.e., after the buyer has paid the price: 'Cut the crops and fruit, and deliver the land and the tree', because the buyer's property is occupied by the seller's property, so it is upon him to vacate and deliver it, just as if there were goods placed on it. And al-Shafi'i said: it is left until the fruit becomes ripe—i.e., animals or humans can benefit from it—and the crop is harvested, because the required delivery is the customary one, and customarily it is not cut like that. And it becomes as when a lease period ends and there is unripe crop on the land, it is left until harvest. And Malik and Ahmad said the same. Our evidence is that delivery is obligatory by the contract, unlike lease, because it is for usufruct, so it is left for an equivalent rent. =
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This lengthy section from Kanz al-Daqa'iq (with commentary) lays out the Hanafi rules on what automatically transfers in a sale of land or a house without explicit mention. The guiding principle is that anything permanently attached for stability (اتصال قرار) is included, while movable items are not unless a custom or condition includes them. The section begins with keys and locks: if the lock is fixed to the door (like a wooden bolt), the keys enter the sale; if the lock is separate (like a padlock), they do not. This is by istihsan, because keys are useless without locks. Next, buildings and structures like stairs, millstones, wells, and gardens are included by virtue of the hadith 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it...'. Trees on land are automatically included (even if small or fruitless), but dry trees are not. Crops and fruits are not included unless explicitly mentioned or conditioned, because they are connected for separation (for harvest), not for stability. However, if the crop is underground (like leeks) and remains for years, it is like a tree and enters. A 'tattimah' lists items included by custom: clothes of slaves (unless fancy), camel nose-ring, donkey rope, packsaddle (but not saddle), and offspring of animals if brought with the mother to the sale. Finally, the passage records a dispute with al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad: they say the buyer must leave the fruit and crops until ripe/harvest, because customary delivery does not require immediate cutting. The Hanafis respond that delivery (taslim) is an obligation of the contract, so the seller must vacate the property immediately; this differs from a lease where the land is for usufruct.

Key Terms
تأبير (ta'bir): Pollination of date palms by inserting male pollen into female flower
تبع (tabi'): Something that follows the main sold item in the contract, entering without separate mention
اتصال الشجر (ittisal al-shajar): Connection of trees to land for stability

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a buyer purchases land with unpollinated fruit on the trees and does not stipulate the fruit?
A) The fruit automatically belongs to the buyer because it is not yet pollinated.
B) The fruit belongs to the seller, and the buyer may demand it be removed immediately.
C) The fruit belongs to the seller, but they may leave it until ripe.
D) The fruit is divided equally between buyer and seller.

Key Takeaway
Trees are automatically included in land sales; crops and fruits require explicit stipulation, and the seller must immediately remove them after sale, unlike the other schools that allow them to mature.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Custom-Based Inclusions: Clothes, Animal Gear, and Offspring
Lists items included by custom in a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which movable items are automatically included in a sale due to custom (urf) according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between items that are customarily included and those that require explicit mention.

Core Idea
Custom decides the inclusion of movables like the clothes of a slave, the rope of a donkey, and the offspring of animals if brought with the mother to the sale.

Not everything that comes with a sold animal or slave is automatically part of the deal. The Hanafi school uses custom (urf) to determine what is considered an appurtenance. This lesson covers the 'tattimah' section of our passage, which lists specific items that are included by custom: the clothes of a slave (unless fancy), the nose-ring of a camel, the rope around a donkey's neck, and the offspring of animals if they accompany the mother to the place of sale. You will learn why the saddle is excluded but the packsaddle is included.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sold item and its associated movables
First determine the primary object of sale (e.g., a slave, a camel, a horse). Then list items that are typically used with it (clothes, gear, offspring).
2
Apply the test of custom (urf)
Check whether customarily such items are considered part of the sold item. For example, the clothes worn by a slave are customarily included unless they are fine garments.
Hanafis rely on urf as a secondary source; custom can override the default rule that movables are not included.
3
Distinguish between items included and excluded by custom
Items like the nose-ring of a camel, the rope around a donkey's neck, and the packsaddle are included. The saddle (for riding) is not included because it is customarily seen as separate.
Reason: the saddle is often expensive and used independently, whereas a packsaddle is closely associated with carrying goods.
4
Apply the rule for offspring of animals
If the offspring is brought with the mother to the sale location, it enters by custom. If not, it remains with the seller.
This applies to camels, mares, donkeys, cows, and goats.

(عيني، فتح بلا تسمية إلا إذا ينبت ولا قيمة له فيدخل في الأصح، وكذا إذا لم ينبت بعد. (عيني) ولا الثمر إلخ لأن اتصال الزرع والثمر ليس للقرار، فصارا كالمتاع الموضوع في الدار، وعبر ههنا بـ"الشرط" وفي الزرع بـ"التسمية؛ ليفيد عدم الفرق، وأن هذا الشرط غير مفسد وخصه بالثمر اتباعا لقوله عليه: "من اشترى أرضا فيها نخل فالثمرة للبائع إلا أن يشترط المبتاع من غير فصل بين المؤبر وغيره، ويقوم مقام التسمية، والشرط ما لو قال: "بكل قليل وكثير هو فيها" إلا أن يبينه بقوله : من حقوقها أو من مرافقها؛ لأن الحق يذكر منه كالطريق والشرب والمرافق ما يرتفق به كمسيل الماء. " هو تبع لابد للمبيع . والتأبير: أن يشق وعاء نحل أنثى، فيجعل فيه شيء من طلع نخل ذكر وهو إصلاح للثمر ، وعند الثلاثة: لو لم يكن الثمرة مؤبرة تدخل، وإلا فلا وفي القياس يدخل الزرع والثمر، وكان وجه القياس بالنظر لمطلق الاتصال بالمبيع، وإن لم يكن على وجه القرار. (عيني، فتح) بالشرط: لأن اتصالهما للقرار فصار كالمتاع الموضوع في الدار. ويقال للبائع إلخ: أي بعد نقد المشتري الثمن : اقطع الزرع والثمرة، وسلم الأرض والشجرة؛ لأن ملك المشتري مشغول بملك البائع، فكان عليه تفريغه وتسليمه، كما إذا كان فيه متاع موضوع، وقال الشافعي: يترك حتى يظهر صلاح الثمرة - أي ينتفع به الدواب أو الإنسان - ويستحصد الزرع؛ لأن الواجب هو التسليم المعتاد، وفي العادة أن لا يقطع كذلك، وصار كما إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض زرع لم يدرك، فإنه يترك إلى الحصاد، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا أن التسليم واجب بحكم العقد، بخلاف الإجارة؛ لأنها للانتفاع، فيترك بأجر المثل، =
Section: On explaining what enters the sale without mention and what does not enter, and other things. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 7/2] And al-mafatih: plural of miftah, keys of locks not keys of padlocks, and what is connected to the building enters. (T, Mustakhlas) Meem-Ya: In a sale of a house now, in custom the name 'house' applies to the vacant land and the building (Ayni) meaning when the keys and locks are of wood, i.e., connected to the door, and if they are of iron not connected to it, it does not enter by consensus. The gist is that what is connected to the house is a dependent and enters the sale, like the attached stairs and the lower millstone built in the house, and the well and its pulley, and likewise the garden that is in it even if large. So if the lock is connected to the door and fixed in it like the kilon and dabbah, which are broad gold or broad silver placed on the face of the door, then [their] keys enter; if the lock is not fixed in it like a padlock, it does not enter due to lack of connection, nor the keys. And by qiyas the keys would not enter because they are not part of the named sold item, nor are they connected. The basis of istihsan is that keys are dependent on locks, since neither can be used without the other. Then know that what enters the sale as a dependent does not have a share of the price unless specified. And the entry of the building in the sale of a house is due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it to the sky and what is below it to the depths.' (Ayni, Madan, Fath) [Page 258] The Book of Sales. 257. What enters the sale. And trees [enter] in the sale of land without mention, and crops do not enter in the sale of land without specification, i.e., trees enter, and fruit does not enter in the sale of trees except by condition, and it is said to the seller: i.e., fruit does not enter in both cases. And 'trees etc.': i.e., absolutely as a dependent, even if small or non-fruit-bearing, unless it is dry, because after dryness it is firewood, not trees. And his statement 'without mention' relates to his statement 'enters' in both issues, i.e., without mention of these things in the sale. The principle in this chapter is that everything connected to the sold item with a connection of stability enters the sale, otherwise not unless custom includes it, like keys and stairs attached to the building enter even if of wood, and what is not attached does not enter. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 7/2, 8) (Fath) 'without mention': refers to both issues because it is connected to them for stability, so it becomes like the building. (Ayni) 'Crops etc.': because they are connected to them for separation by humans, so it does not apply to the pregnancy of a slave girl because that is by God's separation. And if the crop is buried in the ground like leeks, it enters in an absolute sale according to the correct view, because it remains for years like trees, unlike what is above ground. And roses, mulberry leaves, myrtle, and the like are like fruits. Completion: The clothes of a slave boy and slave girl enter the sale without condition because of custom, unless they are fine clothes worn for display at the time of sale, in which case they do not enter without condition. And the nose-ring of a camel, the rope around a donkey's neck, the packsaddle and the saddle blanket enter because of custom, unlike the saddle. And the offspring of a she-camel, a white mare, a female donkey, a cow, and a goat, if taken with the mother to the place of sale, enter because of custom, otherwise not. (Ayni, Fath) Without specification unless it is growing and has no value, then it enters according to the most correct; likewise if it has not yet grown. (Ayni) 'And not fruit etc.' because the connection of crops and fruit is not for stability, so they become like goods placed in the house. He used 'condition' here and 'specification' for crops to indicate there is no difference, and that this condition is not invalidating. He specified fruit following his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever buys land containing palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates [otherwise]', without distinguishing between pollinated and unpollinated. And it stands in place of specification; and the condition is if he says, 'With every little and much that is in it', unless he specifies by saying 'from its rights or from its appurtenances'; because rights are mentioned from it like the path and drinking place, and appurtenances are what one benefits from like the watercourse. It is a dependent inseparable from the sold item. And al-ta'bir: that one splits the vessel of a female date palm and puts some pollen from a male date palm into it, and it is improvement of fruit. According to the three [imams], if the fruit is not pollinated it enters, otherwise not. By qiyas, crops and fruit would enter, and the basis of qiyas is considering absolute connection to the sold item even if not for stability. (Ayni, Fath) 'By condition': because their connection is for stability, so they become like goods placed in the house. 'And it is said to the seller etc.': i.e., after the buyer has paid the price: 'Cut the crops and fruit, and deliver the land and the tree', because the buyer's property is occupied by the seller's property, so it is upon him to vacate and deliver it, just as if there were goods placed on it. And al-Shafi'i said: it is left until the fruit becomes ripe—i.e., animals or humans can benefit from it—and the crop is harvested, because the required delivery is the customary one, and customarily it is not cut like that. And it becomes as when a lease period ends and there is unripe crop on the land, it is left until harvest. And Malik and Ahmad said the same. Our evidence is that delivery is obligatory by the contract, unlike lease, because it is for usufruct, so it is left for an equivalent rent. =
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This lengthy section from Kanz al-Daqa'iq (with commentary) lays out the Hanafi rules on what automatically transfers in a sale of land or a house without explicit mention. The guiding principle is that anything permanently attached for stability (اتصال قرار) is included, while movable items are not unless a custom or condition includes them. The section begins with keys and locks: if the lock is fixed to the door (like a wooden bolt), the keys enter the sale; if the lock is separate (like a padlock), they do not. This is by istihsan, because keys are useless without locks. Next, buildings and structures like stairs, millstones, wells, and gardens are included by virtue of the hadith 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it...'. Trees on land are automatically included (even if small or fruitless), but dry trees are not. Crops and fruits are not included unless explicitly mentioned or conditioned, because they are connected for separation (for harvest), not for stability. However, if the crop is underground (like leeks) and remains for years, it is like a tree and enters. A 'tattimah' lists items included by custom: clothes of slaves (unless fancy), camel nose-ring, donkey rope, packsaddle (but not saddle), and offspring of animals if brought with the mother to the sale. Finally, the passage records a dispute with al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad: they say the buyer must leave the fruit and crops until ripe/harvest, because customary delivery does not require immediate cutting. The Hanafis respond that delivery (taslim) is an obligation of the contract, so the seller must vacate the property immediately; this differs from a lease where the land is for usufruct.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Customary practice that has legal consideration in determining what is included in a sale
تبع (tabi'): Something that follows the main sold item in the contract, entering without separate mention
خطام (khitam): Nose-ring or halter of a camel/donkey

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following items is automatically included in the sale of a donkey by custom?
A) A new saddle.
B) The rope around the donkey's neck.
C) A fancy blanket used for display.
D) The donkey's offspring that stayed at home.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) determines the inclusion of movable items in a sale; the Hanafi school provides specific examples like clothes of a slave (unless fancy), animal gear (nose-ring, rope, packsaddle, not saddle), and offspring if present.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
What Enters a Sale of Land: Trees, Crops, Fruits, and Custom
Explains default inclusion of trees and exclusion of crops/fruits in land sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi default rule for inclusion of trees, crops, and fruits in a land sale, and APPLY the principle of permanent connection (ittisal qarar) and custom to determine inclusion.

Core Idea
Trees enter a land sale automatically; crops and fruits do not—only permanent connection counts.

When you buy a piece of land, what else do you get? Does the sale include the trees growing on it? The crops ready to harvest? The fruit hanging from branches? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer depends on whether the item is permanently attached to the land (ittisal qarar) or merely placed there temporarily. This lesson unpacks the default rules, the role of explicit mention, and the influence of custom. We also examine the hadith that governs fruit inclusion and the disagreement with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Classify items involved in a land sale: land itself, trees (permanent), crops (temporary vegetation), fruits (temporary produce).
Trees include even small or non-fruit-bearing ones; dry trees are excluded.
2
Apply the principle of ittisal qarar
Items with permanent connection (e.g., trees, buildings) enter automatically; those with temporary connection (crops, fruits) do not.
Connection for permanence (qarar) vs. connection for separation (like crops for harvest).
3
Consider explicit mention and custom
Crops and fruits require tasmia (naming) or shart (condition) to be included. Custom can also bring in certain items (e.g., keys, ladders, ordinary clothes).
Expensive clothes worn for display are an exception; they need explicit mention.
4
Analyze the hadith and inter-madhhab dispute
The hadith states fruit belongs to seller unless buyer stipulates. Hanafis hold it applies regardless of pollination; three schools differentiate based on ta'bir.
Hanafi response: Hadith makes no distinction; fruit lacks permanent attachment in both cases.

And trees are in the sale of land without mention, and crops do not enter the sale of land without naming, meaning trees enter and fruit does not enter the sale of trees except by condition, and it is said to the seller: meaning fruit does not enter in both cases, and fruits etc.: meaning as a follower absolutely, even if small or non-fruit-bearing, except if it is dry; because after drying it is firewood not a tree. (Fath) 'without mention': refers to both issues; because it is attached to it for permanence, so it becomes like the building. ('Ayni) Crops etc.: because they are attached to it for separation by humans, so the case of a pregnant slave-girl is not analogous; because that is by God's separation. And if the crop is buried in the ground, like leeks, it enters an absolute sale on the correct view; because it remains for years like a tree, not what is visible. And roses, mulberry leaves, myrtle, and the like are like fruits. ('Ayni) 'And not the fruit etc.' because the connection of crops and fruit is not for permanence, so they become like goods placed in the house. And he expressed here with 'condition' and in crops with 'naming' to indicate no difference, and that this condition is not invalidating. And he specifically mentioned fruit following his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever buys land containing palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it' without distinguishing between pollinated and unpollinated. And it stands in the place of naming. And the condition is if he says: 'with every little and much that is in it' unless he clarifies it by saying: 'from its rights or its amenities'; because a right is mentioned from it like the road and drinking water, and amenities are things used for benefit like the watercourse. It is a follower necessary for the sold item. And pollination is: to cut the vessel of a female palm and put into it some pollen from a male palm, and it is improvement of the fruit. And according to the three (imams): if the fruit is not pollinated it enters, otherwise not. And by analogy, crops and fruit would enter, and the basis of analogy is consideration of absolute connection to the sold item, even if not in the manner of permanence. Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on what is included in a sale of land. The text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) and its commentary (from 'Ayni and Fath) establish the Hanafi position: Trees enter the sale of land automatically by virtue of their permanent connection (ittisal qarar). Even small or non-fruit-bearing trees are included, except dry trees which have become firewood. Crops and fruits do not enter automatically because their connection is temporary, meant for harvest. They require explicit mention (tasmia) or condition (shart). Custom ('urf) can also cause certain items to be included, such as keys, ladders, clothes (unless expensive), and harnesses for animals. The passage cites a hadith of the Prophet: 'Whoever buys land containing palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it.' The Hanafi school interprets this as the default rule. There is a dispute with the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) regarding whether pollination (ta'bir) changes the ruling. According to the three, if the fruit is not yet pollinated, it enters the sale; if pollinated, it does not. The Hanafi school holds that pollination makes no difference; the fruit remains with the seller unless stipulated. The reasoning is that the connection of fruit is not for permanence, so it does not transfer automatically. Analogy (qiyas) would suggest crops and fruit enter due to mere connection, but that is overridden by istihsan based on the hadith and the nature of the connection.

Key Terms
اتصال قرار (ittisal qarar): Permanent connection that makes an item part of the principal object, like trees in land.
تسمية (tasmia): Explicit naming of an item in the sale contract to include it.
تأبير (ta'bir): Pollination of date palms by transferring male pollen to female flowers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following enters the sale of land automatically without explicit mention?
A) A crop of wheat that is ripe and ready to harvest
B) A young palm tree that has not yet produced fruit
C) A dry tree that has become firewood
D) A pile of firewood placed on the land

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses permanent connection (ittisal qarar) to determine what enters a land sale automatically; trees enter, crops and fruits do not, but custom can override.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Seller's Duty to Deliver Vacant Land: Immediate Cutting vs. Leaving Unripe Fruit
Obligation to vacate sold land and inter-madhhab dispute on leaving unripe fruit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires immediate cutting of crops at sale and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using analogy to lease.

Core Idea
Delivery of sold land must be immediate and vacant — custom does not override contractual obligation.

When a land or orchard is sold with standing crops or unripe fruit, a key dispute arises: must the seller immediately cut and clear the land, or may he leave the produce until it ripens? The Hanafi school insists on immediate vacating, while the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools allow the seller to leave the fruit until it becomes usable. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi argument and the counter-argument, focusing on the nature of sale versus lease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling and its rationale
State that the seller must cut and deliver immediately after payment. The rationale is that the buyer's ownership is encumbered by the seller's possession; vacating (tafrig) is a contractual duty.
This applies whether the crops have value or not.
2
State the opposing school's position
The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools hold that the seller may leave the fruit until it ripens, based on custom and analogy to lease (ijarah) where unripe crops are left after the lease term.
They argue that delivery must be customary (taslim muta'arraf).
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes sale from lease: sale transfers ownership immediately, so the seller must vacate; lease transfers usufruct, so leaving the crop is natural. The custom argument fails because the contract's literal requirement overrides custom.
Also, in lease, the landlord is compensated with rent for the delay.
4
Examine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, the buyer can compel immediate removal. Under the other schools, the seller may leave the fruit (and might charge rent for the land during that period).
The dispute affects whether the sale includes a hidden rental component.
5
Apply the ruling to a condition to leave fruit
If the buyer stipulates that the fruit remain on the tree, the Hanafi school holds the sale invalid (fasid) because it introduces an impermissible condition (either a sale in a lease or a loan). The other schools permit it.
This illustrates that the condition contradicts the essence of sale in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ألا ترى أنه لو اشترى دكاناً لا يكون له الطريق إلا بالشرط، وفي الإجارة يدخل من غير شرط، ولا فرق بين ما إذا كان للثمر والزرع قيمة أو لا، ويكون في الحالين للبائع، وأما إذا بذر في الأرض ولم ينبت حتى باع الأرض، فلا تدخل؛ لأنه مودع فيها، ولا يدخلان بذكر الحقوق والمرافق؛ لأنهما ليسا منها، وعكس الثمار والزرع في الحكم الشرب والطريق رمز الحقائق: ۸/۲، ۹] (فتح) اقطعها: أي العين المتصلة بالمبيع الشاملة للزرع والثمر، وفي بعض النسخ بضمير التثنية وهو أولى. (ط، عيني) وسلم المبيع: وهو الشجر والأرض مطلقا سواء كان الزرع والتمر لهما قيمة أو لا. (ط) صح البيع: لأنه مال متقوم منتفع به في الحال أو في المآل] اعلم أن بيع الثمر قبل الظهور لا منتفعاً أن اتفاقا، وإن باعها بعد يصح يصح، خلافاً للشافعي، وإن باعها قبل أن تصير منتفعاً بها بأن لم تصلح لتناول بني آدم وعلف الدواب قيل: لا يجوز، والصحيح أنه يصح، وعند الثلاثة: لا يجوز قبل الإدراك، إلا إذا شرط عدم القطع. (عيني، مسكين) في الحال: تفريغا لملك البائع جبراً، وهذا إذا باع مطلقا أو بشرط القطع. (ط، عيني) فسد: أي البيع؛ لأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وهو شغل ملك الغير، أو هو صفقة في صفقة؛ لأن اشتراط الترك إن كان بأجر كان بيعاً في إجارة، وإن لم يكن بأجر كان بيعاً في إعارة وكذا الزرع بشرط الترك، إلا أنه لو اشتراها مطلقاً وتركها بإذن البائع طاب له الفضل، وبغير إذنه لا يطيب، ويتصدق بما زاد في ذاته، وإن تركها بعد ما تناهى عظمها لم يتصدق بشيء؛ لأن هذا تغير حال لا تحقق زيادة وإن اشتراها مطلقاً ثم استأجر النخيل إلى وقت الإدراك، طاب له الفضل والإجارة باطلة؛ لأنها أضيفت إلى غير محل ،الإجارة، فإن محلها المنفعة دون العين، والباطل معدوم بأصله، فلا يصلح متضمناً، فبقي تضمن الإذن مقصوداً معتبراً، بخلاف ما لو اشترى الزرع واستأجر الأرض إلى أن يدرك حيث يجب أجر المثل، ولا يطيب له الفضل؛ لأن إجارة الأرض متعارفة، لكنها فسدت لجهالة المدة.
والفاسد موجود بأصله معدوم بوصفه فأمكن جعله متضمناً للإذن، وفساد المتضمن يقتضي فساد ما في الضمن، فيفسد الإذن، ويتمكن الخبث ولو اشتراها مطلقاً، فأثمر ثمراً آخر قبل القبض، فسد البيع؛ لعجزه عن التسليم، ولو أثمر بعد القبض يشتركان فيه للاختلاط والقول قول المشتري في مقداره؛ لأنه في يده. وقال العيني والحاصل أن لهذه المسائل ثلاث صور: أحدها: إذا خرج الثمار كله فيجوز بيعه بالاتفاق، وثانيها: أن لا يخرج شيء منه فلا يجوز بيعه بالاتفاق، وثالثها: أن يخرج بعضها دون بعض، فلا يجوز في ظاهر المذهب، وقيل: يجوز إذا كان الخارج أكثر، ويجعل المعدوم تبعاً للموجود استحساناً للضرورة، والأصح أنه لا يجوز، وعند الثلاثة: لا يفسد البيع إذا شرط تركها على الشجر. [ رمز الحقائق: ٩/٢، ١٠] (فتح)
And it is said to the seller: i.e., after the buyer pays the price: Cut the crop and the fruit, and deliver the land and the tree; because the buyer's ownership is occupied by the seller's ownership, so it is upon him to vacate and deliver, as if there were goods placed in it. Al-Shafi'i said: It is left until the fruit appears ripe—i.e., beneficial for animals or humans—and the crop is harvested; because the required delivery is customary delivery, and custom is not to cut like that. And it becomes like when the period of lease expires and there is unripe crop on the land, it is left until harvest. Malik and Ahmad also held this view. Our proof: delivery is obligatory by contract, unlike lease because it is for usufruct, so it is left for rent equivalent. ... [page 259] ... Cut it and deliver the sold item. And whoever sells a bunch of dates whether its ripeness has appeared or not, the sale is valid, and the buyer cuts it immediately. If he stipulates leaving it on the palm trees, the sale is invalid. ... Do you not see that if someone buys a shop, he does not have the right to the road except by condition, while in lease it enters without condition? There is no difference whether the fruit and crop have value or not; in both cases they belong to the seller. As for if he sowed seeds in the land and they have not germinated, then he sold the land, they do not enter; because they are deposited in it. And they do not enter by mentioning rights and amenities because they are not part of them. And the opposite of fruits and crops in ruling is water rights and path. (Fath) 'Cut it': i.e., the physical item connected to the sold item including crop and fruit. (T, 'Ayni) 'And deliver the sold item': i.e., the trees and land absolutely, whether the crop and dates have value or not. (T) 'The sale is valid': because it is valuable property beneficial now or in the future. Know that selling fruit before it appears—no benefit at all—is invalid by consensus. If he sells it after appearance, the sale is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i. If he sells it before it becomes beneficial, i.e., it is not suitable for human consumption or animal fodder, it is said it is not permissible; the correct view is that it is permissible. According to the three, it is not permissible before ripening unless he stipulates not cutting. ( 'Ayni, Miskin) 'Immediately': to vacate the seller's ownership by compulsion. This is if he sells absolutely or with the condition of cutting. (T, 'Ayni) 'Invalid': i.e., the sale; because it is a condition not required by the contract, and it is occupying another's property, or it is a sale in a sale; because stipulating leaving if with rent is a sale in a lease, and if without rent it is a sale in a loan. Likewise crop with condition of leaving. But if he bought it absolutely and left it with the seller's permission, the surplus is permissible for him; without permission it is not permissible, and he gives charity for the increase in itself. If he left it after its size had reached its maximum, he does not give charity; because this is a change of state, not a realized increase. If he bought it absolutely and then leased the palm trees until the time of ripening, the surplus is permissible for him, and the lease is invalid; because it was added to an improper place of lease, for the subject of lease is usufruct, not the physical asset, and the invalid is non-existent in its essence, so it cannot serve as a container. So the implicit permission remains intended and considered. Unlike if he bought the crop and leased the land until ripening, where equivalent rent is obligatory and the surplus is not permissible; because leasing land is customary, but it was invalid due to ignorance of the period. The invalid exists in its essence but is non-existent in its description, so it can be made a container for permission. The invalidity of the container necessitates the invalidity of what is contained, so the permission becomes invalid and impurity settles. And if he bought it absolutely and then it produced another fruit before delivery, the sale is invalid due to inability to deliver. If it produced after delivery, they share it due to mixing, and the buyer's word is taken regarding its quantity because it is in his possession. Al-'Ayni said: The summary is that these issues have three forms: one: if all the fruit appears, its sale is permitted by consensus; second: if none appears, its sale is not permitted by consensus; third: if some appears and some does not, then it is not permitted in the apparent madhhab, and it is said it is permitted if the appeared part is more, making the non-existent a follower of the existent by istihsan due to necessity, but the correct view is that it is not permitted. According to the three, the sale is not invalid if he stipulated leaving them on the tree. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 9/2, 10] (Fath)
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage deals with the seller's obligation to deliver vacant land when crops or fruits are present, and the rulings on selling fruit before or after ripening. After the buyer pays the price, the seller must cut and remove crops and fruits to deliver the land and trees, because the buyer's ownership is encumbered. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad argue that it should be left until ripe, analogizing to lease where unripe crops remain until harvest. The Hanafi response is that delivery is a direct contractual obligation (due from bay'), unlike lease which is for usufruct. The text then discusses various scenarios: selling dates before ripeness is valid in Hanafi (unlike Shafi'i); stipulating that the fruit remain on the tree invalidates the sale because it is a condition conflicting with the contract. However, if the buyer allows the seller to leave it with permission, the increase is permissible. If the buyer later leases the trees for the fruit to ripen, the lease is invalid but the permission stands. There is also discussion of selling fruit that partially appears; the Hanafi position is that it is not permitted unless the majority has appeared (by istihsan), but the relied-upon view is that it is not permitted. The three schools again differ. The passage also covers cases of new fruit before delivery and after delivery.

Key Terms
تفريغ (tafrig): Vacating the property from the seller's possessions to make it free for the buyer.
فساد البيع (fasad al-bay'): Invalidation of the sale due to a condition that contradicts the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a buyer stipulates that the seller may leave the fruit on the palm trees after purchase?
A) The sale is valid and the fruit remains until ripe
B) The sale is invalid (fasid)
C) The condition is valid if the fruit is unpollinated
D) The option is up to the seller

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, selling land with crops obligates the seller to immediately cut and deliver; a condition to leave the crops invalidates the sale, reflecting the priority of contractual duty over custom.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of Fruit and Crop: When Are They Included?
Ruling on inclusion of fruit and crop in sale of land/trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether fruit and crop are included in the sale of land and trees, and apply the rule that a condition to leave the fruit invalidates the sale.

Core Idea
Fruit not yet appeared remains seller's property; a condition to leave invalidates the sale.

When you buy a piece of land with date palms or crops, does the fruit belong to you automatically? The answer depends on whether the fruit has already appeared. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on what is included in such sales, and why a condition to leave the fruit on the tree makes the contract invalid. We also explore the key disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the additional items
The sale involves land or trees. The additional items are fruit (thamar) and crop (zar'). Determine which is being sold and what is attached.
Fruit is on trees; crop is in the ground.
2
Determine the stage of appearance
If the fruit or crop has already appeared (emerged from the bud or sprouted), it is included in the sale. If not, it remains the seller's property.
This is the threshold for inclusion.
3
Check for a condition to leave (shart al-tark)
If the buyer stipulates that the fruit be left on the tree after sale, the sale becomes invalid (fasid) because the condition is not required by the contract and involves occupying the seller's property.
The buyer must cut immediately unless otherwise agreed with permission.
4
Apply to the three scenarios of fruit emergence
The text divides into: (1) all fruit appeared – sale valid by consensus; (2) none appeared – invalid by consensus; (3) partial appearance – Hanafi default is invalid, but some say valid if emerged is more (istihsan). The relied-upon view is invalidity.
This shows the nuance of istihsan.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school requires fruit to be fully ripe (mushtadd) before sale, and does not invalidate the sale if the condition to leave is made. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
The dispute hinges on interpretation of hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يدخل في البيع دك ف اقطعها وسلّم المبيع. ومن باع المرة بدا صلاحها أو لا، صح البيع، ويقطعها المشتري في الحال وإن شرط تركها على النخيل فسد، . بها المشتري أي ترك الثمرة تصير ألا ترى أنه لو اشترى دكاناً لا يكون له الطريق إلا بالشرط، وفي الإجارة يدخل من غير شرط، ولا فرق بين ما إذا كان للثمر والزرع قيمة أو لا، ويكون في الحالين للبائع، وأما إذا بذر في الأرض ولم ينبت حتى باع الأرض، فلا تدخل؛ لأنه مودع فيها، ولا يدخلان بذكر الحقوق والمرافق؛ لأنهما ليسا منها، وعكس الثمار والزرع في الحكم الشرب والطريق رمز الحقائق: ۸/۲، ۹] (فتح) اقطعها: أي العين المتصلة بالمبيع الشاملة للزرع والثمر، وفي بعض النسخ بضمير التثنية وهو أولى. (ط، عيني) وسلم المبيع: وهو الشجر والأرض مطلقا سواء كان الزرع والتمر لهما قيمة أو لا. (ط) صح البيع: لأنه مال متقوم منتفع به في الحال أو في المآل] اعلم أن بيع الثمر قبل الظهور لا منتفعاً أن اتفاقا، وإن باعها بعد يصح يصح، خلافاً للشافعي، وإن باعها قبل أن تصير منتفعاً بها بأن لم تصلح لتناول بني آدم وعلف الدواب قيل: لا يجوز، والصحيح أنه يصح، وعند الثلاثة: لا يجوز قبل الإدراك، إلا إذا شرط عدم القطع. (عيني، مسكين) في الحال: تفريغا لملك البائع جبراً، وهذا إذا باع مطلقا أو بشرط القطع. (ط، عيني) فسد: أي البيع؛ لأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وهو شغل ملك الغير، أو هو صفقة في صفقة؛ لأن اشتراط الترك إن كان بأجر كان بيعاً في إجارة، وإن لم يكن بأجر كان بيعاً في إعارة وكذا الزرع بشرط الترك، إلا أنه لو اشتراها مطلقاً وتركها بإذن البائع طاب له الفضل، وبغير إذنه لا يطيب، ويتصدق بما زاد في ذاته، وإن تركها بعد ما تناهى عظمها لم يتصدق بشيء؛ لأن هذا تغير حال لا تحقق زيادة وإن اشتراها مطلقاً ثم استأجر النخيل إلى وقت الإدراك، طاب له الفضل والإجارة باطلة؛ لأنها أضيفت إلى غير محل ،الإجارة، فإن محلها المنفعة دون العين، والباطل معدوم بأصله، فلا يصلح متضمناً، فبقي تضمن الإذن مقصوداً معتبراً، بخلاف ما لو اشترى الزرع واستأجر الأرض إلى أن يدرك حيث يجب أجر المثل، ولا يطيب له الفضل؛ لأن إجارة الأرض متعارفة، لكنها فسدت لجهالة المدة. والفاسد موجود بأصله معدوم بوصفه فأمكن جعله متضمناً للإذن، وفساد المتضمن يقتضي فساد ما في الضمن، فيفسد الإذن، ويتمكن الخبث ولو اشتراها مطلقاً، فأثمر ثمراً آخر قبل القبض، فسد البيع؛ لعجزه عن التسليم، ولو أثمر بعد القبض يشتركان فيه للاختلاط والقول قول المشتري في مقداره؛ لأنه في يده. وقال العيني والحاصل أن لهذه المسائل ثلاث صور: أحدها: إذا خرج الثمار كله فيجوز بيعه بالاتفاق، وثانيها: أن لا يخرج شيء منه فلا يجوز بيعه بالاتفاق، وثالثها: أن يخرج بعضها دون بعض، فلا يجوز في ظاهر المذهب، وقيل: يجوز إذا كان الخارج أكثر، ويجعل المعدوم تبعاً للموجود استحساناً للضرورة، والأصح أنه لا يجوز، وعند الثلاثة: لا يفسد البيع إذا شرط تركها على الشجر. [ رمز الحقائق: ٩/٢، ١٠] (فتح)
What enters the sale? Cut it and deliver the sold item. Whoever sells the date palm whether its ripeness has appeared or not, the sale is valid, and the buyer cuts it immediately, and if he stipulates to leave it on the palm, it becomes invalid. Do you not see that if someone buys a shop, he does not have the road except by stipulation, while in lease it enters without stipulation. There is no difference whether the fruit and crop have value or not, and in both cases they belong to the seller. As for if he sowed in the land and it did not sprout until he sold the land, it is not included because it is deposited therein. And they are not included by mentioning rights and amenities because they are not from them. The opposite of fruits and crops in ruling is water and road. Know that the sale of fruit before its appearance is not beneficial by agreement, and if he sells it after, it is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And if he sells it before it becomes beneficial, it is said it is not permissible, and the correct is that it is permissible. According to the three, it is not permissible before ripening unless he stipulates not to cut. In the immediate: to vacate the seller's ownership forcibly, and this is if he sells absolutely or with the condition of cutting. It becomes invalid: i.e. the sale, because it is a condition not required by the contract, and it is occupying the property of another, or it is a sale in a lease. And likewise the crop with the condition of leaving. Except that if he buys it absolutely and leaves it with the seller's permission, the profit is lawful for him, without his permission it is not lawful, and he gives charity for the increase. And if he buys it absolutely and then leases the palms until ripening, the profit is lawful for him and the lease is void. And if he buys it absolutely and it produces other fruit before delivery, the sale becomes invalid due to inability to deliver; if it produces after delivery, they share it due to mixing, and the word is the buyer's regarding its amount. Al-'Ayni said: The summary is that these issues have three forms: first, if all the fruit has emerged, its sale is permissible by consensus; second, if none has emerged, its sale is not permissible by consensus; third, if some has emerged and some not, it is not permissible in the apparent madhhab, and it is said it is permissible if the emerged is more, making the non-existent a follower of the existent by istihsan due to necessity, but the correct is that it is not permissible. According to the three, the sale does not become invalid if he stipulates to leave it on the tree.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the rulings on what is included in the sale of date palms and land. The key point: when selling date palms or land, the fruit and crop are considered part of the sale only if they have already appeared. If they have not appeared, they belong to the seller. If the buyer stipulates to leave the fruit on the tree (i.e., not cut it), the sale becomes invalid because it is a condition that does not conform to the contract and involves occupying the seller's property. The commentary extends to cases where the fruit has partially appeared, leading to a three-scenario analysis. The Hanafi position: selling fruit before it appears is not permissible by consensus, but after appearance it is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i who requires the fruit to be fully ripe. The text also discusses the consequences of leaving fruit with or without permission, and the ruling if additional fruit appears after the sale.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit of date palm or other trees
زرع (zar'): Crop planted in the ground
شرط الترك (shart al-tark): Condition that the buyer leaves the fruit on the tree after sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells a date palm with fruit that has not yet appeared, and the buyer stipulates that the fruit will be left on the tree until it ripens, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) The sale is valid because the fruit will appear later.
B) The sale is invalid because the condition is not required by the contract and occupies the seller's property.
C) The sale is valid only if the fruit appears before the end of the contract session.
D) The sale is suspended until the fruit appears.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fruit and crop are included in the sale of land/trees only if they have already appeared; stipulating to leave them invalidates the sale.

Validity of Exception of Known Weight in Fruit Sales
Ruling on validity of sale with exception of known weight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exception of known weight in a sale and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding its validity.

Core Idea
An exception of a known weight from a sold fruit does not invalidate the sale, as the object is known by reference.

This lesson examines a specific issue in sales (buyu'): whether a seller can exclude a known number of pounds from a pile of fruit being sold. The Hanafi school has a prominent ruling from the zahir al-riwayah that this is valid, but there is a conflicting narration from within the school from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from zahir al-riwayah
Identify that according to the apparent transmission, the sale with an exception of known pounds is valid.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note the narration from al-Hasan that the exception is not permissible, followed by al-Tahawi.
3
Analyze the opposing argument
The opposing view argues that the remainder after exception is unknown, causing jahalah that invalidates the sale.
Jahalah in the subject matter generally invalidates a sale.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that this ignorance does not lead to dispute because the sold item is known by pointing, and ignorance of the exact amount does not prevent validity.
The key distinction is between uncertainty in the object versus uncertainty in its quantity.
5
Distinguish the case of a specific tree
If the exception is of a specific palm tree, it is valid by consensus because the remainder is known by sight.
This shows that the dispute is limited to non-specific exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استثنى منها أرطالاً معلومة صح. ولو استثنى منها : أي من الثمرة المبيعة، سواء كانت مقطوعة أو غير مقطوعة، أرطالاً معلومة، صح البيع في ظاهر الرواية، وهو قول مالك، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن الاستثناء لا يجوز، وإليه ذهب الطحاوي؛ لأن الباقي بعد استثناء المعلوم مجهول، قلنا: هذه الجهالة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة؛ إذ المبيع معلوم بالإشارة، وجهالة قدره لا تمنع الجواز، وقيد باستثناء أرطال معلومة منها؛ لأنه مختلف فيه، وأما إذا استثنى نخلاً معيناً فإنه يجوز بالاتفاق؛ لأن الباقي معلوم بالمشاهدة. [ رمز الحقائق: ١٠/٢] (فتح) صح: البيع؛ لأن المبيع معلوم بالإشارة؛ والمستثنى معلوم بالعبارة. (عيني)
And if he excludes from it known pounds, it is valid. And if he excludes from it, i.e., from the sold fruit, whether cut or uncut, known pounds, the sale is valid in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), and it is the statement of Malik. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the exception is not permissible, and al-Tahawi agreed with it because the remainder after the exception of the known is unknown. We say: this ignorance does not lead to dispute because the sold item is known by pointing, and ignorance of its amount does not prevent validity. And it is restricted to excluding known pounds because it is disputed. As for if he excludes a specific palm, it is permissible by consensus because the remainder is known by sight. (Fath) The sale is valid because the sold item is known by pointing and the excepted item is known by expression.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage rules on the validity of selling fruit (e.g., dates) with an exception of a specific number of pounds. According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) from Abu Hanifa, this is valid, and it is also the view of Malik. However, there is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad that Abu Hanifa disallowed it, and al-Tahawi adopted that view, reasoning that the remainder after exception is unknown. The Hanafi response is that the uncertainty does not lead to dispute because the sold fruit is known by pointing (i.e., the tree or pile is specified), and the ignorance of the exact amount does not prevent validity. If the exception is of a specific tree, it is permissible by consensus because the remainder is known by sight.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception: excluding a part of the sold item from the sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, is it valid to sell a pile of dates with an exception of 10 pounds?
A) No, because the remainder is unknown.
B) Yes, in the zahir al-riwayah.
C) Yes, only if the buyer specifies the exact tree.
D) No, because exception is not allowed in sales.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the exception of a known weight from a sold fruit because the sold item is known by reference, and the uncertainty about the remainder does not lead to dispute.

Validity of Selling Grain in Its Husks: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on validity of selling grain in husk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits the sale of grain in the husk while the Shafi'i school prohibits it, and evaluate the evidence each side presents.

Core Idea
A sale is valid if the item exists and is beneficial property—even if hidden—contrary to the Shafi'i condition of full visibility.

When you buy fresh beans still in their pod, is that a valid sale? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree on this everyday transaction. This lesson unpacks their arguments, focusing on whether the item's hidden nature invalidates the contract.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: selling wheat in ear and beans in pod is valid (sahih).
The item exists as valuable property (mal mutaqawwim).
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the item is hidden (gha'ib 'an al-basar), analogous to non-existent items.
He analogizes to milk in udder and oil in olive.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that the item exists but is covered, unlike the analogized cases where nothing exists.
They also reinterpret the hadith as forbidding salam, not spot sale.
4
See the practical difference
Hanafis allow immediate sale of green beans, nuts, etc.; Shafi'is defer until husked.
This difference extends to many covered grains.
5
Understand the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) due to the strength of their evidence.
The Shafi'i analogy is rejected because existence vs. non-existence is a clear distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كبيع برّ في سنبله، وباقلا في قشره. كبيع بر: أي كصحة بيع بر إذا بيع بخلاف جنسه كشعير أو دراهم. (نقاية وباقلا : الباقلا بالمد والتخفيف وبالقصر والتشديد، هو الحب المعروف، وقيل: الباقلا هو الفول وكذا الأرز والسمسم وقال الشافعي: لا يصح بيع الباقلاء الأخضر؛ لأن المعقود عليه غائب عن البصر فلا يجوز، كبيع بذر البطيخ وحب القطن واللبن في الضرع والزيت في الزيتون قبل الاستخراج ولنا أنه مال متقوم منتفع به فيجوز بيعه وعلى هذا الخلاف الفستق والبندق واللوز والجوز والحمص الأخضر وسائر الحبوب المغلفة. مستور "إذا منع ) فإن قلت: روى المسلم وأحمد وغيرهما: أنه عليه فهى عن بيع النخل حتى تزهو، وعن بيع السنبل حتى يبيض ويأمن العاهة، قلت: المراد به السلم يعني لا يجوز السلم فيه حتى يوجد بين الناس، ألا ترى إلى ما رواه البخاري ومسلم عن النبي . الله الثمرة فبم تستحل مال أخيك؟ فيكون حجة لنا في اشتراط وجود المسلم فيه من حين العقد إلى حين المحل، ولو أجري على إطلاقه كان حجة لنا أيضاً في هذا الموضع؛ لأنه يقتضي جواز بيعه بعد ما مطلقاً . من غير قيد بالفرك، ولو كان كما قاله الشافعي لقال: حتى يفرك. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠/٢] فالشافعي قاسه على عدم جواز بيع السمن في اللبن والشيرج في السمسم بجامع أن المبيع معدوم، ولنا: أنه موجود، أنه مستور؛ فلهذا خيرناه بعد الاستخراج بخلاف المقيس عليه؛ فإنه لا وجود له أصلا، فافترقا. (فتح)
Like the sale of wheat in its ear, and beans in their husk. Explanation: Like the validity of selling wheat if sold against a different genus like barley or dirhams. And beans: it is the known grain, and it is said beans are fava beans, and similarly rice and sesame. Al-Shafi'i said: The sale of green beans is not valid because the contracted item is hidden from sight, like the sale of watermelon seeds, cotton seeds, milk in udder, oil in olives before extraction. Our proof: It is valuable property that is beneficial, so its sale is valid. This difference applies to pistachios, hazelnuts, almonds, walnuts, green chickpeas, and all covered grains. If you say: Muslim and Ahmad and others narrated that he forbade selling dates until they ripen, and selling ears until they become white and safe from blight. I say: This refers to salam (advance sale), meaning salam is not permissible until it exists among people. Do you not see what al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated from the Prophet: 'The fruit, how do you make lawful the wealth of your brother?' So it is a proof for us in requiring the existence of the sold item from the time of contract to the time of delivery. And if taken absolutely, it would be a proof for us in this place too, because it implies the permissibility of selling it after it has appeared absolutely, without restriction of threshing. If it were as al-Shafi'i said, he would have said: until it is threshed. Al-Shafi'i analogized it to the impermissibility of selling butter in milk and sesame oil in sesame due to the non-existence of the sold item. Our response: It exists, it is covered; therefore, we allow it after extraction, unlike the analogized case where there is no existence at all.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of selling grains that are still in their husks, such as wheat in the ear and beans in the pod. The Hanafi position is that such sales are valid because the item exists (it is present, though hidden). Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the item is unseen and therefore the sale is like selling non-existent objects (e.g., milk in the udder). The Hanafi response explains that the item actually exists, and its being covered does not prevent it from being valuable property. The hadith prohibiting sale of dates until they ripen is interpreted by Hanafis to apply to salam (advance sale), not to spot sales. The disagreement extends to many similar items like nuts and green chickpeas.

Key Terms
بر في سنبله (burr fi sunbulih): Wheat still in its ear
باقلاء في قشره (baqilla' fi qishrih): Beans in their pod
غائب عن البصر (gha'ib 'an al-basar): Hidden from sight; an item not visible at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, is it valid to sell fresh beans still in their pod?
A) No, because the beans are not visible.
B) Yes, because they are existing property that is beneficial.
C) No, because the Prophet forbade selling dates until they ripen.
D) Yes, only if the seller guarantees extraction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling grain in its husk because the item exists as valuable property; the Shafi'i prohibition based on hiddenness is rejected by distinguishing existence from mere visibility.

Ruling on Responsibility for Weighing and Price Payment Fees
Ruling on who bears the cost of measuring and weighing in a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on who bears the cost of measuring the sold item and identify the two narrations regarding the fee for weighing the price.

Core Idea
The seller pays for measuring the item; the fee for checking your money has two Hanafi answers.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on transaction expenses. When you buy something that needs measuring or weighing, the seller must bear that cost. But when it comes to verifying the money you pay, there is an internal debate within the Hanafi school. Let's unpack the rule and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline rule
Read the first part of the passage: the cost of the measurer, weigher, surveyor, and counter is upon the seller. This is because these actions are part of complete delivery.
This is the default ruling without controversy.
2
Identify the problematic case
Now look at the second part: the fee for weighing the price (the money). The text mentions two narrations from Imam Muhammad. One puts it on the seller unconditionally; another differentiates based on whether the buyer has taken possession.
The dispute is only about the price weighing fee, not the measuring of the sold item.
3
Examine the basis of each narration
Narration 1 (Ibn Sam'a): The fee is on the seller because payment occurs after delivery and weighing. Narration 2 (Ibn Rustam): After the buyer takes possession (qabd), the fee is on the seller; before possession, it is on the buyer.
The reasoning for the second narration: after possession, the buyer guarantees the price, so checking it is his responsibility.
4
Note the distinction between the two fees
The passage explicitly states that the fee for weighing the sold item (the measurer's fee) is separate from the fee for weighing the price. The dispute applies only to the price weighing fee.
Custom nowadays may treat them differently, as the text says 'العرف الآن بخلافه'.
5
Determine the practical impact
In fatwa, the mufta bihi may follow custom. If the local custom places the price weighing fee on the seller or the buyer, that ruling is adopted.
This is an example of how custom can influence a ruling within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البائع الكيال على البائع، وأجرة نقد الثمن ووزنه. الكيال: والوزان والذراع والعداد إذا باع بشرط الكيل والوزن والذرع والعدة. (ط) ابيض غايته . على البائع: لأن هذه الأشياء من تمام التسليم، وهو على البائع، فكذا تمامه. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠/٢] وأجرة نقد الثمن إلخ وهذا رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد وفي رواية ابن رستم عنه أجرة نقد الثمن على البائع؛ لأن النقد يكون بعد التسليم والوزن، وعن محمد: أن أجرة النقد على البائع بعد القبض؛ لأنه إذا قبض دخل في ضمانه بالقبض، وقبل القبض على المشترى والحاصل أن اختلاف الروايتين خاص بأجرة النقد فقط، أما أجرة الوزن فعلى المشتري رواية واحدة، وهذا يقتضي أن أجرة النقد غير أجرة الوزن والعرف الآن بخلافه. (عيني، فتح)
The measurer is upon the seller, and the fee for paying the price and its weighing. The measurer, weigher, surveyor, and counter, if he sells with the condition of measuring, weighing, surveying, and counting, these are upon the seller because they are part of complete delivery. And the fee for paying the price etc. This is the narration of Ibn Sam'a from Muhammad, and in the narration of Ibn Rustam from him, the fee for paying the price is upon the seller because payment occurs after delivery and weighing. And from Muhammad: the fee for paying is upon the seller after receipt (qabd) because when he takes possession, he enters into guarantee by receipt, and before receipt it is upon the buyer. The summary: the difference between the two narrations is specific to the fee for paying the price only; as for the fee for weighing, it is upon the seller in one narration. This implies that the fee for paying is different from the fee for weighing, but custom now is contrary.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage rules on who bears the costs related to the sale transaction. The cost of the measurer, weigher, surveyor, and counter is upon the seller because these are necessary to complete delivery of the sold item. For the cost of weighing the price (i.e., the money paid), there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Ibn Sam'a's narration from Muhammad, the fee for weighing the price is upon the seller because payment comes after delivery and weighing. According to Ibn Rustam's narration, it depends: after the buyer takes possession (qabd), the fee is upon the seller, meaning before possession it is upon the buyer. The text concludes that the fee for weighing the price is distinct from the fee for weighing the item, but custom nowadays may be different.

Key Terms
الكيال (al-kayyal): The measurer, the person who measures the sold item
أجرة نقد الثمن (ujrat naqd al-thaman): The fee for weighing or counting the money paid by the buyer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, who bears the cost of measuring the sold item (e.g., the measurer's wage)?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Shared equally
D) It depends on custom

Key Takeaway
The seller pays for measuring the item; the fee for checking the buyer's money has two Hanafi answers — one always on seller, one depending on possession.

Exception of Known Pounds from Fruit Sale: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on excluding known pounds from fruit sale with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on excluding known pounds from fruit sale, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the principle of isharah to evaluate sale validity.

Core Idea
Sale is valid if the sold item is known by sight, even if the exact remaining weight is unknown.

When a seller says 'I sell you this pile of fruit except for 10 pounds,' does the contract stand? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between the well-known transmitted position (zahir al-riwaya) and a narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind each position and shows how the principle of isharah (indication) resolves the uncertainty concern.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
According to zahir al-riwaya, what is the ruling if a seller excludes known pounds from the fruit? The text states 'صح البيع في ظاهر الرواية' – the sale is valid.
This is the relied-upon position in the school.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that such an exception is not permissible; al-Tahawi followed this view. Their proof: the remainder after excluding the known becomes unknown (majhul), leading to uncertainty that invalidates the sale.
This is a weaker narration, not part of zahir al-riwaya.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The zahir al-riwaya position replies that this ignorance does not lead to dispute because the sold item is known by indication (isharah) – the buyer sees the fruit. Ignorance of the exact remaining weight does not prevent permissibility.
The key is that the object is identifiable by sight, not exact measurement.
4
Distinguish the consensual case
If the seller excludes a specific palm tree (not pounds), all scholars agree the sale is valid because the remainder is known by sight (mushahadah).
This shows the dispute is limited to cases where the exception is by quantity, not by individual item.
5
Apply the principle of isharah
The ruling teaches that in sales, if the object can be pointed to and identified, the contract is valid even if precise quantity is unknown. This is a practical leniency in fruit and similar bulk sales.
This principle is applied in many Hanafi buyu' rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استثنى منها أرطالاً معلومة صح كبيع برّ في سنبله، وباقلا في قشره، البائع الكيال على البائع، وأجرة نقد الثمن ووزنه لأنه من تمام التسليم تميز الجيد من غيره أي أجرة وزن الثمن أي وكصحة . بيع باقلا إلخ وأجرة ولو استثنى منها : أي من الثمرة المبيعة، سواء كانت مقطوعة أو غير مقطوعة، أرطالاً معلومة، صح البيع في ظاهر الرواية، وهو قول مالك، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن الاستثناء لا يجوز، وإليه ذهب الطحاوي؛ لأن الباقي بعد استثناء المعلوم مجهول، قلنا: هذه الجهالة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة؛ إذ المبيع معلوم بالإشارة، وجهالة قدره لا تمنع الجواز، وقيد باستثناء أرطال معلومة منها؛ لأنه مختلف فيه، وأما إذا استثنى نخلاً معيناً فإنه يجوز بالاتفاق؛ لأن الباقي معلوم بالمشاهدة. [ رمز الحقائق: ١٠/٢] (فتح) صح: البيع؛ لأن المبيع معلوم بالإشارة؛ والمستثنى معلوم بالعبارة. (عيني)
If he excludes from it known pounds, it is valid, like the sale of wheat in its ear, and beans in their husk, the seller, the measurer upon the seller, and the fee for paying the price and weighing it because it is part of complete delivery. 'Distinguishing the good from others' meaning the fee for weighing the price. And as for the validity of selling beans etc. and 'if he excludes from it' means from the fruit sold, whether cut or uncut, known pounds, the sale is valid according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), and it is the saying of Malik, and al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the exception is not permissible, and al-Tahawi held this; because the remainder after excluding the known is unknown. We said: this ignorance does not lead to dispute; since the sold item is known by indication, and ignorance of its quantity does not prevent permissibility. And he restricted it to excluding known pounds from it because it is disputed. As for if he excludes a specific palm tree, it is permissible by consensus; because the remainder is known by sight. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 10/2] (Fath) It is valid: the sale; because the sold is known by indication, and the excepted is known by expression. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of excluding a known quantity of fruit from a sale. The author states that if the seller excludes a known number of pounds from the fruit being sold, the sale is valid according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), which is also the position of Imam Malik. However, Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that such an exception is not permissible, a view also held by al-Tahawi. Their reasoning is that the remaining quantity after the exception becomes unknown, leading to uncertainty (jahalah). The Hanafi response is that this ignorance does not lead to dispute because the sold item is identified by indication (isharah) — the buyer sees the fruit — and ignorance of the exact remaining weight does not prevent permissibility. The ruling is restricted to excluding known pounds; if the seller excludes a specific palm tree, all agree it is valid because the remainder is known by sight (mushahadah). The commentators Fath and Ayni emphasize that the sold is known by indication and the excepted by expression, validating the contract.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception of a specified quantity from the sold item.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
إشارة (isharah): Indication by pointing, making the object identifiable by sight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to zahir al-riwaya, what is the ruling if a seller excludes a known number of pounds from fruit being sold?
A) The sale is invalid because the remainder is unknown.
B) The sale is valid because the sold is known by indication.
C) The sale is valid only if the fruit is uncut.
D) The sale is valid only with the buyer's consent.

Key Takeaway
Exception of a known quantity from a fruit sale is valid in the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwaya) because the sold item is identifiable by sight, and the uncertainty in the remainder does not lead to harmful dispute.
