Sale of Covered Produce: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on selling beans in husk and similar covered produce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling produce in its covering, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi response using textual and analogical arguments.

Core Idea
Covered produce exists legally; concealment does not mean non-existence.

In this lesson, we examine a classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the validity of selling produce while still in its natural covering, such as green beans in their husk. The Hanafi school holds such sales valid because the item is existing valuable property. The Shafi'i school objects on grounds of hiddenness (gha'ib). We will trace the arguments, the Hanafi rebuttal, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
The dispute centers on whether it is valid to sell produce like green beans while still in their husk, or similar covered grains and nuts.
The ruling applies to all 'covered grains' (الحبوب المغلفة).
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school rules the sale valid (sahih) because the item is mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) from which benefit is derived, and it exists even if hidden.
The hadith forbidding sale of ripening dates is interpreted as referring to salam, not spot sales.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i rules the sale invalid because the object of contract is hidden from sight (gha'ib 'an al-basar), leading to uncertainty (gharar). He analogizes it to non-existent items like milk in the udder.
The Shafi'i position relies on a general prohibition of selling gha'ib items unless described.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proofs
The Hanafis respond by distinguishing between existing but hidden items and truly non-existent items. The hadith about fruit ('How do you make your brother's property lawful?') is used to support the condition of existence from contract to delivery, which is satisfied here.
The Hanafis also point out that if the Prophet meant to require threshing, he would have said 'until it is threshed.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Hanafis allow the sale of green beans, nuts, and similar items in their husks without prior threshing. Shafi'is require the covering to be removed before the sale is valid.
The Hanafi view also grants the buyer an option after extraction if a defect appears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كبيع بر: أي كصحة بيع بر إذا بيع بخلاف جنسه كشعير أو دراهم. (نقاية وباقلا : الباقلا بالمد والتخفيف وبالقصر والتشديد، هو الحب المعروف، وقيل: الباقلا هو الفول وكذا الأرز والسمسم وقال الشافعي: لا يصح بيع الباقلاء الأخضر؛ لأن المعقود عليه غائب عن البصر فلا يجوز، كبيع بذر البطيخ وحب القطن واللبن في الضرع والزيت في الزيتون قبل الاستخراج ولنا أنه مال متقوم منتفع به فيجوز بيعه وعلى هذا الخلاف الفستق والبندق واللوز والجوز والحمص الأخضر وسائر الحبوب المغلفة. فإن قلت: روى المسلم وأحمد وغيرهما: أنه عليه فهى عن بيع النخل حتى تزهو، وعن بيع السنبل حتى يبيض ويأمن العاهة، قلت: المراد به السلم يعني لا يجوز السلم فيه حتى يوجد بين الناس، ألا ترى إلى ما رواه البخاري ومسلم عن النبي . الله الثمرة فبم تستحل مال أخيك؟ فيكون حجة لنا في اشتراط وجود المسلم فيه من حين العقد إلى حين المحل، ولو أجري على إطلاقه كان حجة لنا أيضاً في هذا الموضع؛ لأنه يقتضي جواز بيعه بعد ما مطلقاً . من غير قيد بالفرك، ولو كان كما قاله الشافعي لقال: حتى يفرك. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠/٢] فالشافعي قاسه على عدم جواز بيع السمن في اللبن والشيرج في السمسم بجامع أن المبيع معدوم، ولنا: أنه موجود، أنه مستور؛ فلهذا خيرناه بعد الاستخراج بخلاف المقيس عليه؛ فإنه لا وجود له أصلا، فافترقا. (فتح)
Like the sale of wheat: meaning like the validity of selling wheat when sold against its genus, like barley or dirhams. (Niqaya) And 'baaqila': beans, with elongation and lightening and with shortening and doubling, it is the known grain, and it is said baqila is fava beans, likewise rice and sesame. And al-Shafi'i said: The sale of green beans is not valid because the object of contract is hidden from sight, so it is not permissible, like the sale of watermelon seeds, cotton seeds, milk in the udder, oil in olives before extraction. And for us: it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) from which benefit is derived, so its sale is permissible. And on this disagreement are pistachios, hazelnuts, almonds, walnuts, green chickpeas, and all covered grains. If you say: Muslim and Ahmad and others narrated that he (peace be upon him) forbade the sale of dates until they ripen, and the sale of ears until they turn white and are safe from blight. I say: what is meant is salam (forward sale); meaning it is not permissible in salam until it exists among people. Do you not see what al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about fruit: 'How do you make your brother's property lawful?' So it would be an argument for us regarding the condition of existence of the sold item from the time of contract until the time of delivery. And if it were applied absolutely, it would be an argument for us in this place as well; because it implies the permissibility of selling it after that absolutely, without the condition of threshing. And if it were as al-Shafi'i said, he would have said 'until it is threshed.' [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 10/2] So al-Shafi'i analogized it to the impermissibility of selling butter in milk and sesame oil in sesame seeds, on the basis that the sold item is non-existent. And for us: it exists, it is hidden; therefore we give him the choice after extraction, contrary to the analogized case; for there is no existence at all, so they differ. (Fath)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage addresses the dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the sale of produce that is still in its natural covering, such as green beans in their husk, wheat in its ear, and similar covered grains. The Hanafi position is that such sales are valid because the commodity is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) from which benefit can be derived, and its existence is established even if concealed. Al-Shafi'i argues that it is invalid because the object of sale is hidden from sight (gha'ib 'an al-basar), leading to uncertainty. The Hanafi response counters that the item is physically present, though covered; therefore, it is existing. The text then addresses a potential objection based on hadiths that forbid selling dates until they ripen and ears until they turn white. The Hanafi scholar explains that these hadiths refer to salam (forward sale), not spot sales. The hadith about fruit (narrated by Bukhari and Muslim) is actually used to support the Hanafi position because it requires the sold item to exist from contract to delivery — a condition that is met here. The Hanafis further argue that if the prohibition were as broad as al-Shafi'i claims, the Prophet would have said 'until it is threshed,' but he did not. Finally, al-Shafi'i's analogy (qiyas) to butter in milk and oil in sesame seeds is rejected because in those cases, the item is non-existent (ma'dum), whereas here it exists but is concealed (mastur). Therefore, the two cases are different. This controversy applies to all covered grains like pistachios, almonds, walnuts, etc.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and permissible to benefit from.
غائب عن البصر (gha'ib 'an al-basar): Hidden from sight, not visible at the time of contract.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a specified future delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the sale of green beans in their husk valid despite being hidden?
A) Because the seller discloses the quantity.
B) Because the item is existing, even if hidden, and is valuable property.
C) Because the buyer has the option to return it after extraction.
D) Because the hadith forbids only the sale of dates, not beans.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a covered produce is a valid subject of sale because it exists as valuable property; concealment does not equate to non-existence.

Expenses of Weighing and Payment: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on allocation of expenses for weighing and payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi narrations regarding the fee for paying the price and identify the default rule for weighing/measuring costs.

Core Idea
Seller bears weighing costs; payment fee depends on possession timing.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq deals with the allocation of expenses for weighing, measuring, and paying the price in a sale. The general principle is that the seller must ensure complete delivery, which includes weighing. However, there is a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the fee for paying the price, with two narrations from Imam Muhammad regarding whether it falls on the buyer or seller based on possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for weighing/measuring
Note that the text states: weighing, measuring, cubit-measuring, and counting are part of complete delivery (tam al-taslim), so their expenses are on the seller.
This is the default position.
2
Understand the basis: complete delivery
The seller is obligated to deliver the sold item fully, which includes any necessary measurements. Hence, the costs fall on the seller.
This is consistent with the Hanafi principle of 'al-taslim'.
3
Distinguish the fee for payment from weighing
The text separates 'ujrat al-naqd' (fee for paying the price) from weighing/measuring fees. The dispute only applies to the payment fee.
Do not conflate the two.
4
Contrast the two narrations
Ibn Sama'a narrates from Muhammad: payment fee is always on the seller. Ibn Rustam narrates from Muhammad: payment fee is on the buyer before qabd (possession) and on the seller after qabd.
Both are valid riwayat from the same Imam.
5
Note the role of custom and final commentary
Al-Ayni notes that in his time, custom merged the payment fee with weighing fee, so the practical ruling may follow custom.
Custom ('urf) can determine the operative rule when narrations differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيال: والوزان والذراع والعداد إذا باع بشرط الكيل والوزن والذرع والعدة. (ط) ابيض غايته . على البائع: لأن هذه الأشياء من تمام التسليم، وهو على البائع، فكذا تمامه. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠/٢] وأجرة نقد الثمن إلخ وهذا رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد وفي رواية ابن رستم عنه أجرة نقد الثمن على البائع؛ لأن النقد يكون بعد التسليم والوزن، وعن محمد: أن أجرة النقد على البائع بعد القبض؛ لأنه إذا قبض دخل في ضمانه بالقبض، وقبل القبض على المشترى والحاصل أن اختلاف الروايتين خاص بأجرة النقد فقط، أما أجرة الوزن فعلى المشتري رواية واحدة، وهذا يقتضي أن أجرة النقد غير أجرة الوزن والعرف الآن بخلافه. (عيني، فتح)
The measurer: and the weigher, the cubit-measurer, and the counter when he sells on condition of measuring, weighing, cubit-measuring, and counting. (T) ... upon the seller: because these things are part of complete delivery, and delivery is upon the seller, so likewise its completion. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 10/2] And the fee for paying the price, etc. And this is the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, and in the narration of Ibn Rustam from him, the fee for paying the price is upon the seller; because the payment occurs after delivery and weighing. And from Muhammad: that the fee for payment is upon the seller after possession; because when he takes possession, he enters into his guarantee by possession, and before possession it is upon the buyer. The upshot is that the difference between the two narrations is specific to the fee for payment only. As for the fee for weighing, it is upon the buyer in one narration, and this implies that the fee for payment is different from the fee for weighing, and the custom now is contrary to this. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses who bears the costs associated with weighing, measuring, counting, and paying the price in a sale. The general principle is that the seller is responsible for complete delivery (tam al-taslim), which includes weighing and measuring; therefore, the fees for these services fall on the seller. However, there is a nuance regarding the fee for paying the price (naqd al-thaman). According to the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Imam Muhammad, the fee for payment is on the seller because payment occurs after delivery and weighing. In the narration of Ibn Rustam from Muhammad, the fee for payment is on the seller after the buyer takes possession (qabd); before possession, it is on the buyer. The key point is that this disagreement only concerns the fee for payment. The fee for weighing (ajrat al-wazn) is consistently on the buyer according to one narration. The text notes that the custom (urf) in the author's time differed, making payment fee part of weighing fee. This is an intra-Hanafi difference where both narrations are recorded from Imam Muhammad, and the choice depends on the prevailing custom.

Key Terms
تمام التسليم (tam al-taslim): Complete delivery of the sold item, including all necessary actions for transfer.
أجرة النقد (ujrat al-naqd): Fee for paying the price, typically for counting or verifying coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in what situation is the fee for paying the price on the buyer?
A) Always, because the buyer must pay.
B) Before possession, according to the narration of Ibn Rustam.
C) After possession, according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a.
D) It is always on the seller.

Key Takeaway
The seller bears weighing/measuring costs as part of complete delivery; the fee for payment is subject to two Hanafi narrations depending on possession timing.

Order of Delivery in a Sale: Buyer Delivers Price First
Ruling on order of delivery between buyer and seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the order of delivery in a sale, explain the legal reasoning, and distinguish the Shafi'i counter-position.

Core Idea
The buyer delivers the price first because ownership transfers by contract, but the seller's right is secured by payment.

In a sale where the item is present, who hands over first? The Hanafi school rules that the buyer must pay first, based on the contract's effect. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the conflict with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the specific legal directive: the buyer must deliver the price first if the sold item is present.
This is the main rule, with exceptions.
2
Understand the rationale
Analyze the reasoning: ownership passes by contract, but guarantee (daman) depends on possession. Paying first establishes the seller's right and achieves equality between the parties.
The text states: 'في دفع الثمن ليتعين حق البائع بالقبض تحقيقاً للمساواة'.
3
Note the condition
The rule applies when the sold item is present (hader). If the item is absent (gha'ib), the buyer may refrain from paying until the seller produces it.
This is an exception that protects the buyer.
4
Recognize the dispute
Contrast with the Shafi'i view: they hold that both parties exchange simultaneously.
The text notes: 'وعند الشافعي يتقابضان معا'.
5
Apply the exceptions
In barter (commodity for commodity) or sale of price for price, the parties deliver together.
The text says: 'سلما معا لاستوائهما في التعيين'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على المشتري. ومن باع سلعة بثمن سلّمه أولاً، وإلا سلّما معا. أي سلم المشتري الثمن على المشتري: لأن عليه تسليم الجيد والوزن من تمام التسليم. (عيني) سلمه لأن حق العقد لدخوله في ملكه بالعقد، وإن كان تقرر الضمان عليه يتوقف على القبض، فيدفع الثمن ليتعين حق البائع بالقبض تحقيقاً للمساواة، فلهذا يؤمر المشتري بتسليم الثمن أولاً إذا كان المبيع حاضراً، وإن كان غائباً فللمشتري أن يمتنع من تسليم الثمن حتى يحضر البائع المبيع، فلو اشتراه بشرط أن يدفع المبيع أولاً فسد البيع؛ لأنه لا يقتضيه العقد، وإذا سلم المشتري الثمن أولاً، فللبائع أن يسلم المبيع حين يستوفي الثمن، إلا أن يكون مؤجلاً. (فتح، جوهري) أولا : لتعيين حق البائع فيه وعند الشافعي يتقابضان معا. (عيني، كافي) بيع وإلا : أي وإن لم يكن ! سلعة بثمن، بل كان بيع ثمن بثمن أو بيع سلعة بسلعة. (عيني) سلما معا لاستوائهما في التعيين إذا كان بيع سلعة بسلعة أو كان المبيع دينا والثمن سلعة، أو في عدم التعيين إذا بيع ثمن بثمن، ثم التسليم يكون بالتخلية على وجه يتمكن من القبض بلا مانع ولا حائل، وأن يقول: كان خليت بينك وبين المبيع، فلو لم يقله أو كان بعيداً لم يصر قابضاً. (فتح)
Upon the buyer. And whoever sells a commodity for a price, he delivers it first, otherwise they deliver together. Meaning, the buyer delivers the price, upon the buyer: because upon him is the delivery of the good quality and weight, which is part of complete delivery. (Ayni) He delivers it because the right of the contract is his entering into ownership by the contract, although the establishment of guarantee upon him depends on possession. So he pays the price to establish the seller's right by possession, achieving equality. Therefore, the buyer is ordered to deliver the price first if the sold item is present. If it is absent, the buyer may refrain from delivering the price until the seller brings the sold item. If he buys it on condition that the seller delivers the item first, the sale becomes invalid because it is not required by the contract. And when the buyer delivers the price first, the seller may deliver the item when he collects the price, unless it is deferred. (Fath, Jawhari) First: to establish the seller's right in it. And according to al-Shafi'i, they exchange together. (Ayni, Kafi) And 'otherwise' meaning if it is not a commodity for price, but rather a sale of price for price or a sale of commodity for commodity. (Ayni) They deliver together because they are equal in specification if it is a sale of commodity for commodity or if the sold is a debt and the price a commodity, or in lack of specification if sold is price for price. Then delivery is by vacating (takhliya) in a way that he is able to take possession without obstacle or barrier, and he says: 'I have vacated between you and the sold item.' If he does not say it or is far, he does not become a possessor. (Fath)
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains the order of delivery between buyer and seller in a sale. The general rule is that the buyer must deliver the price first if the sold item is present (hader). This is because the contract gives the buyer ownership, but guarantee (daman) depends on possession. By paying the price first, the seller's right is established and equality is achieved. If the sold item is absent (gha'ib), the buyer may withhold payment until the seller produces the item. If the buyer stipulates that the seller deliver first, the sale becomes invalid (fasid) because it contradicts the contract's demands. Once the buyer pays, the seller may deliver the item when he receives the price, unless the payment is deferred. The Shafi'i school holds that they should exchange simultaneously (yataqabidan ma'an). In cases of barter (commodity for commodity) or sale of price for price, they deliver together because both are equally specified or unspecified. The mode of delivery is vacating (takhliya) with a verbal statement, enabling the buyer to take possession without barriers.

Key Terms
تسليم (taslim): Delivery of the sold item or price from one party to the other.
تخلية (takhliya): Vacating the sold item so the buyer can take possession without hindrance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a present item for a price, what does the Hanafi school say about the order of delivery?
A) The seller must deliver the item first.
B) The buyer must deliver the price first.
C) They both deliver simultaneously.
D) The order depends on the nature of the goods.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer delivers the price first when the sold item is present, based on the contract's effect on ownership and guarantee.

Option of Stipulation: The Three-Day Limit
Definition and ruling on khiyar al-shart with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum valid period for khiyar al-shart in the Hanafi school, identify the primary textual evidence, and explain why exceeding three days invalidates the sale.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-shart is valid only up to three days; longer periods invalidate the sale entirely.

In the Hanafi school, a sale contract is binding unless the parties agree to an option of stipulation. But does that option have a time limit? Yes — and it is surprisingly short. This lesson unpacks the three-day maximum, its proof from the hadith of Hibban ibn Munqidh, and why the same ruling is shared by major Imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
From the passage: option of stipulation is valid for three days or less; if more, it is not valid. Note that the rule applies whether the option is for one party, both, or a third party.
The period must be known; absolute or perpetual options are invalid by consensus.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told Hibban ibn Munqidh: 'When you sell, say: no deception between me and you, and I have the option for three days.'
This is a hadith ahad, with zanni thubut and zanni dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's permission to stipulate a three-day option creates an exception to the general rule that sales must be immediately binding. The explicit mention of 'three days' limits the exception.
The imperative 'fa qul... wa li al-khiyar' indicates permissibility, and the specification of three days is taken as the maximum.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and tarjih
Abu Hanifa, Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik all agree that more than three days is invalid. The three-day allowance is a concession based on the hadith; beyond that, the original analogy (qiyas) applies, making it invalid.
This is a rare instance of cross-madhhab agreement on a specific limit.
5
Apply to modern practice
In a real estate sale, if a buyer and seller agree to a 10-day inspection period, the Hanafi view is that the sale is invalid because the option exceeds three days. The parties must either reduce the period or use a different mechanism.
Some jurisdictions allow longer inspection periods; a Hanafi mufti would advise to limit to three days or structure the transaction differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار الشرط أي في بيان أحكامه صح للمتبايعين، ولأحدهما خيار ثلاثة أيام أو أقل، ولو أكثر لا، أي العاقدين كليهما باب خيار الشرط المصدر بمعنى اسم المفعول يعني الخيار المشروط أو إضافة الخيار إليه إضافة الحكم إلى سببه. (مسكين فتح] البيع نوعان: لازم وغير ،لازم فلما بين اللازم شرع في بيان غير اللازم، وهو ما فيه خيار شرط أو رؤية أو عيب وهذه الثلاثة مذكورة في المختصرات ومبوب في هذا الكتاب وخيارات أخر تبلغ أسبابها إلى ستة عشر كخيار تعيين وغين ونقد وكمية واستحقاق وتقرير فعلي وكشف حال وخيانة في مرابحة وتولية، وفوات وصف مرغوب فيه وتتفريق صفقة بهلاك بعض مبيع وإجازة عقد فضولي وظهور المبيع مستأجراً أو مرهوناً والفسخ بإقالة وتحالف وقدم خيار الشرط على البواقي، لكونه أعم وجودا؛ لأنه يجري في غير البيع أيضاً، وقد نظم في النهر ما يصح فيه خيار الشرط وما لا يصح فقال: يأتي خيار الشرط في الإجارة والبيع والإبراء والكفالة والرهن والعتق وترك الشفعة والصلح والخلع مع الحوالة والوقف والقسمة لا الصرف والإقرار والوكالة وأيمان وهذا يغتنم والإقالة ولا النكاح والطلاق والسلم نذر وكذا يصح في الكتابة، وينبغي صحته في المزارعة والمعاملة؛ لأنهما إجارة، وإنما لا يصح في النكاح والطلاق واليمين والنذر والوكالة والإقرار؛ لأنه إنما يدخل في لازم يحتمل الفسخ، فلهذا لا يصح في الوصية؛ لأنها غير لازمة، وإنما قدم الخيار على سائر الخيارات؛ لأنه يمنع ابتداء الحكم، وإنما كان عمله في منع الحكم دون السبب؛ لأن حقه أن من لا يدخل في البيع؛ لكونه في معنى القمار، ولكن لما جاء به السنة لم يكن بد من العمل به، فأظهرنا عمله في منع الحكم تقليلا لعمله بقدر الإمكان؛ لأن دخوله في السبب يستلزم الدخول في الحكم دون العكس. (فتح، عناية) صح: أي خيار الشرط ولو بعد العقد. (عيني) ولأحدهما : أي لأحد المتبايعين في مبيع، كله أو بعضه كثلثه أو ربعه . (ط، عيني) خيار ثلاثة أيام: لقوله عليها لحبان منقذ بن عمرو الأنصاري، وكان يغبن في البياعات: فقال عليها: "إذا بايعت فقل: لا خلابة بيني وبينك ولي الخيار ثلاثة أيام" رمز الحقائق: ۱۱/۲] فالبيع بخيار الشرط أربعة أوجه: خيار البائع منفردا، وخيار المشتري منفرداً، وخيارهما مجتمعاً، وخيار غيرهما، والخيار إما أن يكون مطلقاً أو موبداً أو موقتاً، والأولان لا يجوزان بالاتفاق، وأما الموقت فيجوز بشرط أن يكون الوقت معلوماً، فلو كان مجهولاً لم يصح اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح) أقل من ثلاثة أيام وفسد عند الإطلاق. (عيني) ولو أكثر لا [ أي لو شرطا أكثر من ثلاثة أيام. (ط)] أي لا يصح الخيار عند أبي حنيفة، وبه قال زفر والشافعي ومالك؛ لأنه مخالف لمقتضى العقد، ولكن جوزنا إلى الثلاثة للحديث المذكور، فبقي ما وراءه على ظاهر القياس،
Chapter of Option of Stipulation: that is, explaining its rulings. It is valid for both parties to the sale, and for one of them: an option of three days or less; if more, it is not valid. Both parties to the contract. Chapter of Option of Stipulation: the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle, meaning the stipulated option, or the addition of the option to it is the addition of the ruling to its cause. (Miskin, Fath) Sales are of two types: binding and non-binding. When the binding was explained, he proceeded to explain the non-binding, which includes option of stipulation, option of sight, and option of defect. These three are mentioned in the abridged works and are chaptered in this book. There are other options whose causes reach sixteen, such as option of specification, option of payment, option of quantity, etc. The option of stipulation is given precedence due to its wider scope; it also occurs in non-sales. The poem in al-Nahr states what is valid for option of stipulation and what is not: 'The option of stipulation occurs in: lease, sale, quittance, guarantee, pledge, manumission, relinquishment of pre-emption, compromise, khul', with hawala, endowment, division. Not in: currency exchange, acknowledgement, agency, oaths – and this is worth noting; and in retraction of a sale (iqalah), nor in marriage, divorce, salam, vow. Likewise, it is valid in kitaba (contract of manumission), and it should be valid in muzara'ah and mu'amalah because they are leases. It is not valid in marriage, divorce, oaths, vows, agency, acknowledgement, because it enters only into a binding contract that admits rescission. Hence it is not valid in a will because it is non-binding. The option is given precedence because it prevents the beginning of the ruling. Its operation is in preventing the ruling, not the cause. (Fath, Inayah) It is valid: meaning the option of stipulation, even after the contract. (Ayni) For one of them: meaning for one of the two parties in the sale, in all or part of the sold item, like a third or a fourth. (T, Ayni) Option of three days: due to his statement, peace be upon him, to Hibban ibn Munqidh al-Ansari, who was cheated in sales: he said, peace be upon him: 'When you sell, say: no deception between me and you, and I have the option for three days.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 11/2) Thus sale with option of stipulation has four aspects: option of the seller alone, option of the buyer alone, option of both together, and option of a third party. The option is either absolute, perpetual, or temporary. The first two are not permissible by consensus. The temporary is permissible provided the time is known; if unknown, it is not valid by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) Less than three days, and it is invalid when unspecified. (Ayni) And if more, it is not [meaning if they stipulate more than three days]. That is, the option is not valid according to Abu Hanifa; Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik also said this; because it contradicts the requirement of the contract, but we allowed up to three days due to the mentioned hadith, so beyond that remains on the apparent analogy.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is the chapter on the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). It begins by stating that the option is valid for both or one of the parties for three days or less; more than three is invalid. The commentary explains that 'option' here means the stipulated option, and it is a non-binding sale. The text lists sixteen other types of options but prioritizes this one due to its wider application. It then presents a poem from al-Nahr enumerating contracts where option of stipulation is valid (e.g., lease, sale, guarantee) and where it is not (e.g., marriage, divorce, currency exchange). The reasoning is that the option only enters into binding contracts that admit rescission; non-binding contracts like wills do not allow it. The passage then returns to the basic ruling: the option is valid for three days based on the hadith of Hibban ibn Munqidh, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) advised him to stipulate a three-day option to avoid deception. The chapter explains that the option can be for the seller, buyer, both, or a third party, and it must be temporary with a known time; absolute or perpetual options are invalid by consensus. If less than three days are stipulated but unspecified, the sale is invalid. The ruling on more than three days: Abu Hanifa, Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik all hold it invalid because it contradicts the nature of a sale contract; the three-day concession is due to the specific hadith, and beyond that, the original analogy (qiyas) applies, making it invalid.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The right to rescind or confirm a contract within a stipulated period, granted by mutual agreement.
الخيار المطلق (al-khiyar al-mutlaq): An unconditional option without a fixed time; invalid by consensus.
الخيار المؤبد (al-khiyar al-mu'abbad): A perpetual option with no end; invalid by consensus.
الخيار الموقت (al-khiyar al-muwaqqat): A temporary option with a known time period; valid in Hanafi school up to three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum valid period for khiyar al-shart?
A) One day
B) Three days
C) Seven days
D) No limit

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-shart is valid for a known period of three days or less; exceeding three days invalidates the sale because it contradicts the contract's binding nature and the hadith-based exception.

Dispute on Extending Khiyar al-Shart Beyond Three Days
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the maximum duration of khiyar al-shart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the duration of khiyar al-shart, identify the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa limits khiyar al-shart to three days; the Sahibayn allow any known period — a classic clash between textual limit and purposive reasoning.

The khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) allows a buyer or seller to rescind a contract within a fixed period. But how long can that period be? Abu Hanifa holds a strict three-day limit based on explicit hadith, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, argue that any known duration is permissible to achieve the option's purpose of avoiding deception. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the school's final position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core disagreement
Abu Hanifa says khiyar al-shart cannot exceed three days; the Sahibayn say any known period is valid. Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one initially.
The Sahibayn differ from their teacher, which is common in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ invalidated a four-day option and declared 'The option is three days.' Also, a hadith of Habban mentions three days. These are explicit textual limits.
The hadith is dalil naqli, considered stronger than analogy.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the purpose of khiyar is to prevent deception, which may require longer than three days. They cite the practice of Ibn Umar, who permitted options up to two months.
They also rely on istihsan (juristic preference) to extend the period.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa responds that Ibn Umar's practice may refer to other types of options (khiyar al-ru'yah or al-'ayb), not khiyar al-shart. The explicit hadith overrides the general purpose.
This shows how textual evidence is prioritized over teleological reasoning in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah al-khilaf
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: if more than three days are stipulated, the contract is corrupt unless ratified within three days. The Sahibayn's view is marjuh. The practical consequence is whether a longer option period is enforceable.
Al-Hidayah and later authorities affirm Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text

وقالا تجوز الزيادة إذا ذكرا مدة معلومة طالت أو قصرت وبه قال أحمد؛ لأنه شرع نظراً للمتعاقدين للاحتراز عن الغبن، وقد لا يحصل ذلك في ثلاثة أيام رمز الحقائق: ۱۱/۲] ولأن ابن عمر ما أجازه إلى شهرين، وله: أنه ورد من رواية عبد الرزاق عن أنس له أن رجلاً اشترى بعيراً وشرط الخيار أربعة أيام، فأبطل عليها البيع، وقال: "الخيار ثلاثة أيام، وما روي عن ابن عمر ما ليس بنص في خيار الشرط، فيحتمل أن يكون خيار الرؤية أو العيب. (عيني، فتح) فإن أجاز إلخ يحتمل أن يراد بالصحة عود العقد إلى الجواز بعد أن كان فاسدا؛ لأن المفسد قد ارتفع قبل تقرره، وهذا قول العراقيين من أصحابنا، ويحتمل أن تكون الصحة بمعنى النفاذ، فيراد بها ما يقابل الموقوف، بناء على ما ذهب إليه أهل خراسان من أن العقد مع شرط الخيار أكثر من ثلاث موقوف، إن أجيز العقد في الثلاث ظهر أن لا فساد في العقد؛ لعدم وجود المفسد، وإن مضى جزء من اليوم الرابع قبل الإجازة فهو فاسد وهذا الوجه أوجه، وقال زفر والشافعي: العقد فاسد، فلا ينقلب جائزاً، قلنا: إنه إسقاط للمفسد قبل تقرره فيجوز [رمز الحقائق: ۱۲/۲] (فتح) صح: [ أي البيع والخيار عندنا استحساناً. (معدن)] وقال زفر: لا يصح؛ لأنه بيع شرطت فيه الإقالة الفاسدة، فالصحيحة مما تفسده مثل أن يقول: بعتك هذا بشرط أن تقيل البيع؛ لكونه خلاف العقد فاشتراط الفاسدة أولى قالت الثلاثة وهو القياس وجه الاستحسان ما أن روي ابن عمر باع ناقة بهذا الشرط، و لم ينكر عليه أحد . من الصحابة؛ لأنه في معنى خيار الشرط؛ إذ الحاجة مست إلى الانفساخ عند عدم النقد تحرزاً المماطلة فألحق به [ رمز الحقائق: ١٢/٢] (فتح) وإلى أربعة لا : [ أي ولو باع على أنه إن لم ينقد الثمن إلى أربعة أيام أو أكثر فلا بيع. (ط، عيني)] أي لا البيع عند أبي حنيفة بناء على أصله وعن أبي يوسف ،روايتان والأصح أنه يوافق الإمام، وعن محمد يجوز ذلك بناء على أصله، ولكن أبا يوسف أجاز الزيادة في شرط الخيار عملاً بالأثر، وهو ما روي عن ابن عمر لما أنه أجاز الخيار إلى شهرين، وعمل في هذه المسألة بالقياس [ رمز الحقائق: ١٢/٢] فإن نقد إلخ: لأنه إسقاط للمفسد قبل تقرره، وقال زفر: انعقد فاسداً، فلا ينقلب جائزاً، وبه قال الشافعي، ودعوى الزيلعي الإجماع في هذه المسألة غير صحيح إلا أن يراد إجماع الإمام مع صاحبيه، ثم هذه المسألة على وجوه: إما أن لا يبينا الوقت أو بينا وقتاً مجهولاً بأن قال: إن لم ينقده أياما، أو بينا وقتاً معلوماً وهو ثلاثة أيام، فالعقد في هذه الصور كلها فاسد، إلا أن ينقد في الثلاث لما قلنا، وإن بينا وقتاً معلوماً وهو ثلاثة أيام أو دونه، فإنه يجوز. [رمز الحقائق: ١٢/٢]
And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The increase is permissible if they mention a known period, whether long or short. Ahmad also said that, because it is legislated as a consideration for the contracting parties to avoid deception, and that may not be achieved in three days (Ramz al-Haqaiq 11/2). And because Ibn Umar permitted it up to two months. And for him (Abu Hanifa): it is reported from the narration of Abd al-Razzaq from Anas that a man bought a camel and stipulated an option of four days, so he (the Prophet) invalidated the sale and said: 'The option is three days.' And what is reported from Ibn Umar is not definitive regarding khiyar al-shart; it could be option of inspection or defect. (Ayni, Fath) 'If he ratifies, etc.': It is possible that what is meant by validity is the return of the contract to permissibility after it was corrupt, because the corrupting element was removed before it was established. This is the saying of the Iraqis among our companions. And it is possible that validity means effectiveness (nafadh), opposite of suspended, based on what the Khurasanis held: that the contract with stipulation of option more than three is suspended; if the contract is ratified within three, it appears there was no corruption because no corrupting element existed; if a part of the fourth day passes before ratification, it is corrupt. This view is more appropriate. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: the contract is corrupt and cannot become permissible. We say: it is a removal of the corrupting element before its establishment, so it is permissible. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 12/2) (Fath) 'Sahha': i.e., the sale and option are valid by istihsan in our view. (Madan) Zufar said: it is not valid because it is a sale in which a corrupt iqalah is stipulated; even a valid iqalah corrupts it, like if one says: I sell you this on condition that you rescind the sale, because it contradicts the contract, so stipulating a corrupt one is more deserving. The three (Imams) said: it is the analogy. The basis of istihsan is that Ibn Umar sold a she-camel with this condition and no one among the companions objected to him, because it is in the meaning of khiyar al-shart, as there is need for rescission when payment is not made to avoid procrastination, so it was annexed to it. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 12/2) (Fath) 'And to four, not': i.e., if he sold on condition that if he does not pay the price within four days or more, there is no sale. (T, Ayni) i.e., the sale is not valid according to Abu Hanifa based on his principle. From Abu Yusuf, there are two narrations, and the more correct is that he agrees with the Imam. From Muhammad, it is permissible based on his principle. However, Abu Yusuf permitted the increase in stipulating the option due to the athar, i.e., what was reported from Ibn Umar that he permitted the option up to two months, and in this issue he acted on analogy. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 12/2) 'If he pays, etc.': because it is a removal of the corrupting element before its establishment. Zufar said: it was contracted as corrupt, so it cannot become permissible. Al-Shafi'i also said that. The claim of al-Zayla'i of consensus on this issue is incorrect unless what is meant is consensus of the Imam with his two companions. Then this issue has several aspects: either they do not specify the time, or they specify an unknown time by saying: if he does not pay him some days, or they specify a known time, i.e., three days. In all these forms the contract is corrupt unless he pays within the three, as we said. If they specify a known time of three days or less, then it is permissible. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 12/2)
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether khiyar al-shart can exceed three days. Abu Hanifa holds that stipulating more than three days invalidates the option and the sale becomes corrupt, because the hadith limits it to three days. The two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), hold that any known period is permissible, based on the rationale that the purpose is to avoid deception, which may require longer than three days. They cite the practice of Ibn Umar who permitted options up to two months. Abu Hanifa counters with a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ invalidated a four-day option, and argues that Ibn Umar's case may have been about khiyar al-ru'yah or al-'ayb, not khiyar al-shart. The text then discusses the meaning of 'validity' (sihha) when a contract is ratified within three days: the Iraqi scholars say the contract returns to permissibility after being corrupt, while the Khurasanis say the contract was merely suspended and becomes effective retrospectively. The latter view is preferred. Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold the contract is absolutely corrupt and cannot become valid, but the Hanafis respond that the corrupting element is removed before it is established. The passage then examines a specific scenario: if a seller stipulates that if the price is not paid within a specified period, the sale is void. This is valid by istihsan because it is analogous to khiyar al-shart, as both serve to prevent hardship. The dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad is detailed: for a condition of four days, Abu Hanifa says the sale is invalid; from Abu Yusuf there are two narrations, the more correct being agreement with Abu Hanifa; Muhammad allows it. The passage concludes with the rule that if the time is unspecified or unknown, the contract is corrupt unless payment is made within three days; if the time is known as three days or less, it is permissible.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juridical preference that overrides analogy for a stronger reason, such as custom or necessity.
المفسد (al-mufsid): The element that corrupts a contract, rendering it fasid.
الموقوف (al-mawquf): A suspended contract that becomes effective upon ratification.

D) Suspended until payment is made

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, khiyar al-shart is limited to three days per Abu Hanifa's mufta bihi position, though the Sahibayn allow any known period.

Khiyar al-Shart: Duration and Validity of Conditional Sales
Ruling on sale with condition of payment within three days and extension beyond three days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on khiyar al-shart duration, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi position permits any known option period, despite Abu Hanifa's three-day limit.

This lesson examines khiyar al-shart (option of condition) in sales, focusing on the permissible duration. The Hanafi school has an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). We will analyze the textual proofs and the role of istihsan, and see why the later fatwa follows the Sahibayn, allowing any specified period.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and the ikhtilaf
Identify the basic rule: a sale conditioned on payment within three days is valid if payment occurs. For longer periods, Abu Hanifa invalidates, whereas the Sahibayn permit any known duration.
The difference stems from conflicting proofs: a hadith limiting option to three days vs. istihsan based on need and Companion practice.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (Hanafi dissenter) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that such a contract is absolutely corrupt (fasid) and cannot become valid even if payment occurs.
Their basis: a corrupt condition invalidates the contract from the outset, similar to stipulating a cancellation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They use qiyas: if stipulation of valid cancellation corrupts, then stipulation of corrupt cancellation is even more corrupt.
This is a strong qiyasi argument, but the Hanafi response uses istihsan.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the corrupting factor (the condition of cancellation) is removed before it takes effect when payment occurs within the three days. Istihsan is invoked due to need and because Ibn 'Umar practiced it.
This is like removing a defect before it becomes established, analogous to other cases in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, if the period is three days or less, all agree it's valid. For longer periods, Abu Hanifa's ruling would invalidate, but the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) follows the Sahibayn, making any known period valid.
Thus, in contemporary contracts, a 10-day option is valid if the period is specified and known.

فإن نقد إلخ: لأنه إسقاط للمفسد قبل تقرره، وقال زفر: انعقد فاسداً، فلا ينقلب جائزاً، وبه قال الشافعي، ودعوى الزيلعي الإجماع في هذه المسألة غير صحيح إلا أن يراد إجماع الإمام مع صاحبيه، ثم هذه المسألة على وجوه: إما أن لا يبينا الوقت أو بينا وقتاً مجهولاً بأن قال: إن لم ينقده أياما، أو بينا وقتاً معلوماً وهو ثلاثة أيام، فالعقد في هذه الصور كلها فاسد، إلا أن ينقد في الثلاث لما قلنا، وإن بينا وقتاً معلوماً وهو ثلاثة أيام أو دونه، فإنه يجوز. Chapter of Option of Condition. If he ratifies within three [days] it is valid, and if he sells on the condition that if he does not pay the price within three days there is no sale between us, it is valid, and for four [days], no. If he pays within three, it is valid. The seller's option prevents the transfer of the sold item. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: Extension is permissible if they mention a known period, whether long or short, and Ahmad said the same; because it was legislated with consideration for the contracting parties to guard against exploitation, and that may not be achieved in three days. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 11/2] And because Ibn 'Umar permitted it up to two months. And for him [Abu Hanifa]: it came from the narration of 'Abd al-Razzaq from Anas that a man bought a camel and stipulated an option of four days, so he [the Prophet] invalidated the sale and said: "The option is three days." And what is narrated from Ibn 'Umar is not a text regarding option of condition, so it may be option of sight or defect. ('Ayni, Fath) "If he ratifies" etc. It is possible that validity means the return of the contract to permissibility after it was defective, because the corrupting factor was removed before its establishment, and this is the saying of the Iraqis among our companions. And it is possible that validity means effectiveness, i.e., what is opposed to suspended, based on what the Khurasanis held that the contract with an option of more than three is suspended: if it is ratified within three, it becomes clear that there was no corruption in the contract due to the absence of the corrupting factor, and if part of the fourth day passes before ratification, it is corrupt, and this aspect is more appropriate. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The contract is corrupt and cannot become valid. We reply: It is removal of the corrupting factor before its establishment, so it is permissible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 12/2] (Fath) "Valid" [i.e., the sale and the option according to us by istihsan. (Ma'din)] And Zufar said: It is not valid because it is a sale in which a corrupt cancellation is stipulated, so a valid cancellation is more likely to corrupt it, like if one says: I sell you this on condition that you cancel the sale; because it is contrary to the contract, so stipulating a corrupt [cancellation] is more appropriate. The three said: That is qiyas. The basis of istihsan is what is narrated that Ibn 'Umar sold a she-camel with this condition and none of the Companions objected to him; because it is similar to option of condition, as need exists for rescission upon non-payment to prevent procrastination, so it is annexed to it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 12/2] (Fath) "And for four, no": [i.e., if he sells on condition that if he does not pay the price within four days or more there is no sale. (T, 'Ayni)] i.e., the sale is not [valid] according to Abu Hanifa based on his principle. From Abu Yusuf there are two narrations, and the more correct is that he agrees with the Imam. From Muhammad it is permissible based on his principle, but Abu Yusuf permitted increase in option of condition by acting on the athar, which is what is narrated from Ibn 'Umar that he permitted an option up to two months, and he acted in this issue by qiyas. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 12/2] "If he pays" etc.: because it is removal of the corrupting factor before its establishment. Zufar said: It was contracted corruptly and cannot become valid, and al-Shafi'i said the same. The claim of al-Zayla'i of consensus in this issue is incorrect unless it means consensus of the Imam with his two companions. Then this issue has forms: either they do not specify the time, or they specify an unknown time such as saying: if he does not pay it [within] days, or they specify a known time which is three days. In all these forms the contract is corrupt, except if he pays within three for what we said. And if they specify a known time which is three days or less, it is permissible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 12/2]
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from the Chapter on Option of Condition (Khiyar al-Shart) explains the ruling on a sale conditioned on payment within a specific period. The basic ruling is that if a seller says, 'I sell you this on condition that if you do not pay the price within three days, there is no sale,' then the sale is valid if the buyer pays within three days. However, if the condition is for four days or more, the sale is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) permit any specified period, relying on istihsan and the practice of Ibn 'Umar. Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith from Anas where the Prophet invalidated a sale with a four-day option, limiting it to three days. The commentary discusses the reasoning: the option is meant to protect against exploitation, and three days suffices. The Khurasani scholars hold that the contract is suspended; if ratified within three days it becomes valid retrospectively, otherwise it is corrupt. Zufar and al-Shafi'i consider such a contract absolutely corrupt. The Hanafi position (istihsan) is that the corrupting factor (the condition of cancellation) is removed before it becomes effective by payment within three days, so the contract becomes valid. This is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a sale contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference abandoning qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), a sale with an option of seven days is:
A) Invalid because it exceeds three days.
B) Valid if the period is specified and known.
C) Valid only if the buyer pays within three days.
D) Invalid because it is a corrupt condition.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view permits any known option period in khiyar al-shart, following the Sahibayn's istihsan over Abu Hanifa's three-day limit.

Effect of Khiyar on Ownership and Liability
Rules on ownership and liability of the sold item during the option period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on ownership and liability during seller's and buyer's option periods, and apply them to scenarios of destruction and defects.

Core Idea
During khiyar, the buyer does not own the item; liability is based on market value, not price.

When a sale includes an option (khiyar), the ownership and liability for the sold item are not straightforward. The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, distinguishes between different types of options and their effects. This lesson unpacks the rulings on ownership transfer, liability for destruction, and the treatment of defects, highlighting the key intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes. Understanding these principles is crucial for determining the rights and obligations of parties in an option contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main khiyar scenarios
The passage discusses seller's option (khiyar al-ba'i') and buyer's option (khiyar al-mushtari). Note how each affects ownership of the item and the price.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خيار البائع' and 'خيار المشتري'.
2
Trace the Hanafi dispute on buyer ownership during buyer's option
Abu Hanifa holds the buyer does not own; the Sahibayn say he does. Understand the reasoning: avoiding meeting of countervalues vs. avoiding ownerless property.
The parallels (slaves of Ka'bah, estate absorbed by debt) are key to Abu Hanifa's argument.
3
Analyze liability for destruction
If the item is destroyed while in the buyer's possession during seller's option, the buyer pays the market value (qimah), not the price (thaman). Understand why and how this differs from the Shafi'i position.
The distinction between qimah and thaman is critical.
4
Examine the rulings on defects
The passage covers what happens if the item becomes defective in either party's hands. Note the difference between fungible and non-fungible goods regarding compensation for diminution.
The prohibition of riba affects compensation for fungibles.
5
Understand the concept of suspended ownership
Abu Hanifa's view that ownership is suspended during the option period, and when ratified, it traces back to the contract time. This explains why the buyer gets the increments (e.g., offspring).
This addresses the objection of ownership being lost to no owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخيار البائع يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملكه، وبقبض المشتري يهلك بالقيمة، وخيار المشتري لا يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملك البائع ولا يملكه المشتري. أي لا يملك المشتري المبيع عن ملكه؛ لأن تمام البيع لا يكون إلا بالتراضي، ولا يتم الرضا مع الخيار. وفائدة هذا أن البائع إذا أعتق عبده المبيع بالخيار يعتق، ويملك التصرف في المبيع دون المشتري، وإن قبضه بإذن البائع. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۲/۲] وقال الشافعي وأحمد يثبت ملك المشتري في المبيع بالعقد اعتبارا بخيار العيب، من هذا أن خيار المشتري يمنع خروج الثمن عن ملكه، وإذا كان الخيار لهما لا يخرج المبيع ولا الثمن عن ملك المالك، ولم يبين الحكم في الثمن إذا كان الخيار للبائع، فالحكم فيه أنه يخرج عن ملك المشتري بالاتفاق فلا يدخل في ملك البائع عنده، ويدخل عندهما. (نهاية، فتح)
وعرف وبقبض المشتري المبيع الذي فيه خيار البائع إذا هلك في يده في مدة الخيار. (عيني) يهلك بالقيمة: [ أي البدل؛ ليعم المثلي إذا قبضه بإذن البائع. (ط)] مطلقاً سواء قبض بإذن البائع أو بغير إذنه، حتى يلزم المشتري قيمته يوم القبض إن كان من ذوات القيم، وإلا فيجب ضمان مثله إن كان مثلياً، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: يهلك بالثمن مطلقاً، ولو هلك في يد البائع انفسخ البيع، فلا شيء له على المشتري كما في البيع الصحيح المطلق، ولو تعيب في يد المشتري، فللبائع أن يلزم البيع إن شاء، وإن شاء فسخ البيع وضمنه النقصان إذا كان قيمياً. فلو كان مثلياً ليس له تضمينه النقصان لشبهة الربا، ولو تعيب في يد البائع فهو على خياره، ولكن المشتري إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء فسخ كما في البيع المطلق، وإذا كان العيب بفعل البائع تسقط حصته من الثمن؛ لأن ما يحدث بفعله يكون مضموناً عليه رمز الحقائق (۱۲/۲ ، ۱۳] ثم اعلم أن القيمة يطلق على الذي عند التجار أي المالية التي يعرفونها أهل السوق، والثمن يطلق على الذي تقرر بين العاقدين سواء كان أقل من أصل القيمة أو أكثر. (فتح، محشي)
وخيار المشتري لا يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملك البائع، فيخرج المبيع عن ملكه؛ لأن البيع من جهته تام، ولكن لا يملكه المشتري، ولا خلاف أن النفقة تجب على المشتري، وإنما لم يملكه المشتري؛ لئلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك شخص واحد، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : يملكه؛ لئلا يكون الملك زائلاً لا إلى مالك، ولا نظير له، وقال علي: الا سائبة في الإسلام" فلو لم يكن له مالك يكون سائبة، وبه قالت الثلاثة وله أن الخيار شرع للتروية، فلو دخل في ملكه، والثمن لم يخرج عن ملكه اتفاقاً، يلزم اجتماع البدلين في ملكه، أما زوال الملك لا إلى مالك: فله نظائر كعبيد الكعبة يخرجون عن ملك ملاكهم، ولا يدخلون في ملك أحد، وكذا الستر في الكعبة. وكذا التركة المستغرقة بالدين تخرج عن ملك الميت ولا تدخل في ملك الورثة، ولا يكون سائبة؛ لأنا نقول: الحال موقوفة إن أجيز البيع يستند إلى وقت العقد، فيتبين أنه ملكه من ذلك الوقت ولهذا كان له الزوائد، ولا يرد على الإمام ما لو غصب المدبر وأبق من يده فإنه يضمن قيمته، ولا يخرج به عن ملك المالك، فيجتمع العوضان في ملك واحد؛ لأنه ضمان جناية لا معاوضة، وكلامنا في الذي يقبل الانتقال بحكم المعاوضة. (عيني، فتح، عناية غيره)
The seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership, and if the buyer takes possession, it is destroyed at [its] value. The buyer's option does not prevent the sold item from leaving the seller's ownership, nor does it make the buyer own it. i.e., the buyer does not own the sold item from his ownership; because complete sale is only by mutual consent, and consent is not complete with the option. The benefit of this is that if the seller frees his slave that is sold with an option, he is freed, and he has the right to dispose of the sold item, not the buyer, even if he took possession with the seller's permission. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 12/2] Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: The buyer's ownership of the sold item is established by the contract, by analogy to option of defect. From this [it follows] that the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership. And if the option belongs to both, neither the sold item nor the price leaves the ownership of its owner. He did not clarify the ruling on the price when the option belongs to the seller. The ruling is that it leaves the buyer's ownership by agreement, but does not enter the seller's ownership according to him [Abu Hanifa], while it does enter according to them [the Sahibayn]. (Nihaya, Fath) And it is known: "and if the buyer takes possession of the sold item which has an option for the seller, if it is destroyed in his hand during the option period" ('Ayni) "it is destroyed at value": [i.e., the substitute; to include fungibles if he took possession with the seller's permission. (T)] absolutely, whether he took possession with or without the seller's permission, so that the buyer is liable for its value on the day of taking possession if it is of valuables, otherwise he must guarantee its like if it is fungible. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: it is destroyed at the price absolutely. And if it is destroyed in the seller's hand, the sale is rescinded, and nothing is owed by the buyer, as in an absolute valid sale. If it becomes defective in the buyer's hand, the seller may either enforce the sale or rescind and claim compensation for the diminution if it is of valuables. If it is fungible, he cannot claim compensation for diminution due to suspicion of riba. If it becomes defective in the seller's hand, he remains on his option, but the buyer may either take it at full price or rescind, as in an absolute sale. If the defect occurs by the seller's act, his share of the price is dropped because what occurs by his act is guaranteed against him. Ramz al-Haqa'iq (12/2,13) Then know that 'value' (qimah) is applied to what is with merchants, i.e., the market value known to people of the market, while 'price' (thaman) is applied to what is agreed between the contracting parties, whether it is less than or more than the original value. (Fath, Muhashi) And the buyer's option does not prevent the sold item from leaving the seller's ownership, so it leaves his ownership because the sale from his side is complete, but the buyer does not own it. There is no dispute that maintenance expenses are obligatory on the buyer. The reason the buyer does not own it is to avoid the meeting of the substitute and the substituted in the ownership of one person. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two [Sahibayn] said: He does own it, to avoid ownership being lost to no owner, and there is no parallel to that. 'Ali said: "except a sa'ibah in Islam." So if it had no owner, it would be a sa'ibah. The three [Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i] said this. And for him [Abu Hanifa]: The option was legislated for deliberation. If it enters his ownership while the price has not left his ownership by agreement, that would necessitate the meeting of the two countervalues in his ownership. As for the loss of ownership to no owner, it has parallels: like the slaves of the Ka'bah who leave their owners' ownership and do not enter anyone's ownership, and likewise the covering of the Ka'bah, and likewise the estate entirely absorbed by debt leaves the deceased's ownership and does not enter the heirs' ownership. And it is not a sa'ibah because we say: The situation is suspended; if the sale is ratified, it is traced back to the time of contract, and it becomes clear that he owned it from that time, and therefore he is entitled to its increments. And it is not objected against the Imam that if someone seizes a mudabbar and it flees from his hand, he guarantees its value, and it does not leave the owner's ownership, so the two compensations meet in one ownership; because that is a guarantee for injury, not a compensation. Our discussion is about what is subject to transfer by way of compensation. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah, etc.)
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of khiyar (option) on ownership and liability in a sale. When the seller has the option (khiyar al-ba'i'), the sold item remains in the seller's ownership during the option period. If the buyer takes possession and the item is destroyed, the buyer is liable for its value (not just the price), because the transfer of ownership is not complete. The buyer's option (khiyar al-mushtari) does not prevent the item from leaving the seller's ownership—it leaves the seller's ownership but the buyer does not acquire full ownership yet; this is to avoid having both the item and the price in one person's ownership. The Sahibayn and Shafi'i argue that the buyer does acquire ownership, but Abu Hanifa maintains that ownership is suspended. The commentary explains the practical implications: if the seller frees the slave during the option period, it is valid; if the item is destroyed in the buyer's hands, the buyer pays its value; if it is destroyed in the seller's hands, the sale is rescinded. The passage also discusses defects and liability, and distinguishes between value (qimah, market price) and price (thaman, agreed sum). Finally, it addresses the objection that ownership would be lost to no owner, citing parallels such as slaves of the Ka'bah and estates overwhelmed by debt.

Key Terms
قيمة (qimah): Market value of the item, determined by experts; used for liability when destruction occurs before full ownership transfer.
ثمن (thaman): The price agreed upon in the contract, which may differ from market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer takes possession of an item during the seller's option period and the item is destroyed, what is the buyer liable for according to the Hanafi school?
A) The agreed price (thaman).
B) The market value (qimah) at the time of taking possession.
C) Nothing, because the seller still owns it.
D) Double the price as a penalty.

Key Takeaway
During the option period, the buyer does not own the sold item according to Abu Hanifa, and liability for destruction is based on market value, not the contract price.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
6 min
Khiyar al-Shart and Ownership Transfer
Explains how khiyar al-shart affects ownership of seller and buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ownership during khiyar al-shart, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and compare it with the Shafi'i view, identifying the practical consequences.

Core Idea
During khiyar al-shart, the sold item leaves the seller's ownership but does not enter the buyer's – ownership is suspended.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a critical question in sale contracts with a stipulated option period: who owns the item during the option? The Hanafi school has a nuanced position that differs from other schools and even has internal disagreement. Understanding this ruling helps clarify liability, disposal rights, and the legal logic behind ownership transfer in conditional sales.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: during khiyar al-shart, the sold item leaves the seller's ownership but does not enter the buyer's. Note the reasoning: sale is not complete without full consent, which is absent during the option.
Also note the price: if option is for seller, price leaves buyer's ownership but does not enter seller's (Abu Hanifa) or does (Sahibayn).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that ownership transfers immediately upon contract, analogous to khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect).
This is the cross-madhhab dispute.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their dalil is qiyas to khiyar al-'ayb, where ownership transfers immediately and the option is only a right to return.
The Shafi'i view is based on the principle that a valid contract transfers ownership unless there is a specific impediment.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi rebuts the analogy: khiyar al-shart is for deliberation, not for a pre-existing defect. Also, if buyer owned, both item and price would be in his ownership momentarily, combining the two substitutes, which is impermissible.
The Hanafi position is supported by analogies to Ka'bah slaves and debt-ridden estate, where ownership is removed without a new owner.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: if buyer manumits the sold slave during option, Hanafi says invalid (since he doesn't own it), Shafi'i says valid. If item is destroyed in buyer's hand, Hanafi says he pays the value, Shafi'i says he pays the price.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار الشرط
عن ملكه، وبقبض المشتري يهلك بالقيمة، وخيار المشتري لا يمنع ولا يملكه ..... أي لا يملك المشتري المبيع عن ملكه أي ملك البائع؛ لأن تمام البيع لا يكون إلا بالتراضي، ولا يتم الرضا مع الخيار. وفائدة هذا أن البائع إذا أعتق عبده المبيع بالخيار يعتق، ويملك التصرف في المبيع دون المشتري، وإن قبضه بإذن البائع. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۲/۲] وقال الشافعي وأحمد يثبت ملك المشتري في المبيع بالعقد اعتبارا بخيار العيب، من هذا أن خيار المشتري يمنع خروج الثمن عن ملكه، وإذا كان الخيار لهما لا يخرج المبيع ولا الثمن عن ملك المالك، ولم يبين الحكم في الثمن إذا كان الخيار للبائع، فالحكم فيه أنه يخرج عن ملك المشتري بالاتفاق فلا يدخل في ملك البائع عنده، ويدخل عندهما. (نهاية، فتح)
وخيار المشتري لا يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملك البائع، فيخرج المبيع عن ملكه؛ لأن البيع من جهته تام، ولكن لا يملكه المشتري، ولا خلاف أن النفقة تجب على المشتري، وإنما لم يملكه المشتري؛ لئلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك شخص واحد، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : يملكه؛ لئلا يكون الملك زائلاً لا إلى مالك، ولا نظير له، وقال علي: الا سائبة في الإسلام" فلو لم يكن له مالك يكون سائبة، وبه قالت الثلاثة وله أن الخيار شرع للتروية، فلو دخل في ملكه، والثمن لم يخرج عن ملكه اتفاقاً، يلزم اجتماع البدلين في ملكه، أما زوال الملك لا إلى مالك: فله نظائر كعبيد الكعبة يخرجون عن ملك ملاكهم، ولا يدخلون في ملك أحد، وكذا الستر في الكعبة. وكذا التركة المستغرقة بالدين تخرج عن ملك الميت ولا تدخل في ملك الورثة، ولا يكون سائبة؛ لأنا نقول: الحال موقوفة إن أجيز البيع يستند إلى وقت العقد، فيتبين أنه ملكه من ذلك الوقت ولهذا كان له الزوائد، ولا يرد على الإمام ما لو غصب المدبر وأبق من يده فإنه يضمن قيمته، ولا يخرج به عن ملك المالك، فيجتمع العوضان في ملك
واحد؛ لأنه ضمان جناية لا معاوضة، وكلامنا في الذي يقبل الانتقال بحكم المعاوضة. (عيني، فتح، عناية غيره)
Chapter of Option of Stipulation. Regarding his ownership, and by the buyer's taking possession it is destroyed by value, and the buyer's option does not prevent nor does he own it... i.e., the buyer does not own the sold item from his ownership, i.e., the seller's ownership; because complete sale is only by mutual consent, and consent is not complete with the option. The benefit of this is that if the seller manumits his slave who is sold with option, the manumission is valid, and he owns disposal of the sold item without the buyer, even if the buyer took possession with the seller's permission. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 12/2] Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: Ownership of the buyer is established in the sold item by the contract, by analogy to the option of defect. From this, the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership. And if the option is for both, neither the sold item nor the price leaves the owner's ownership. And he did not explain the ruling regarding the price if the option is for the seller. The ruling is that it leaves the buyer's ownership by agreement, but does not enter the seller's ownership according to him (Abu Hanifa), and enters according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). (Nihayah, Fath) And the buyer's option does not prevent the sold item from leaving the seller's ownership, so it leaves his ownership; because the sale from his side is complete, but the buyer does not own it. And there is no dispute that expenses are obligatory on the buyer. He did not make the buyer own it only to avoid the combination of substitute and substituted in the ownership of one person. This is according to Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He owns it, to avoid ownership being removed without an owner, which has no parallel. And Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: except the runaway in Islam. So if it did not have an owner, it would be a runaway. And the three (scholars) said: The option is legislated for deliberation; so if it entered his ownership while the price did not leave his ownership by agreement, the combination of the two substitutes would occur in his ownership. As for removal of ownership without an owner: there are parallels like the slaves of the Ka'bah—they leave the ownership of their owners and do not enter anyone's ownership. Likewise the curtain of the Ka'bah. Likewise the estate overwhelmed by debt leaves the ownership of the deceased and does not enter the ownership of the heirs, and it is not a runaway; because we say: the situation is suspended; if the sale is ratified, it is retroactive to the time of the contract, so it becomes clear that he owned it from that time, and therefore he gets the increments. And the objection against the Imam (Abu Hanifa) regarding the case of a mudabbar who is usurped and then escapes from his hand: he guarantees its value, and it does not thereby leave the owner's ownership, so the two compensations combine in one ownership; that is because it is a guarantee for injury, not a exchange. And our discussion is about what can be transferred by exchange. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah, etc.)
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of khiyar al-shart (the option of stipulation) on the ownership of the sold item and the price. It begins by stating that during the option period, the buyer does not become owner of the item; rather, it leaves the seller's ownership but does not enter the buyer's. The reasoning is that complete consent is lacking until the option is resolved. The practical benefit is that if the seller manumits the slave sold on option, it is valid, and the seller may dispose of the item. The passage then presents the view of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad that the buyer becomes owner immediately at the contract, analogizing to khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). A sub-discussion explains the status of the price: if the option is for the seller, the price leaves the buyer's ownership by agreement, but according to Abu Hanifa it does not enter the seller's ownership, while according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad it does. The passage then elaborates on Abu Hanifa's reasoning that if the buyer owned the item while the price is still in his ownership, the two substitutes would combine. The objection that ownership cannot be without an owner is rebutted by citing cases like slaves of the Ka'bah and the estate overwhelmed by debt. The analogy with a mudabbar's guarantee is distinguished because that guarantee is for injury, not exchange. The passage concludes that the item is in a suspended state until the option is exercised.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The right to cancel or confirm a sale within a stipulated period.
ملك (milk): Ownership; the legal right of possession and disposition.
سائبة (sa'ibah): A runaway slave or animal without an owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens to the ownership of the sold item during khiyar al-shart?
A) It remains with the seller.
B) It transfers to the buyer immediately.
C) It leaves the seller's ownership but does not enter the buyer's.
D) Ownership is shared between seller and buyer.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-shart, the sold item is in a state of suspended ownership: it leaves the seller's domain but does not vest in the buyer until the option is exercised.
