Valid and Invalid Conditions in Sale: Wasf vs Shart Fasid
Rulings on conditions and descriptions in sale contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid descriptions from invalid conditions in a sale contract and state the effect on the contract's validity.

Core Idea
A valid description is known and free from uncertainty; an invalid one involves gharar and makes the sale fasid.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules on stipulating descriptions in sales. The key is whether the description is known (wasf) or involves uncertainty (gharar). The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq gives clear examples to distinguish valid from corrupt conditions.

Guided Path
1
State the Rule
Identify the central rule: any description without gharar is valid to stipulate; any with gharar is invalid and makes the sale fasid.
2
Identify Valid Descriptions
Recognize descriptions that are concrete and known, like 'writer' or 'milker', as valid (wasf).
These are known traits at the time of sale.
3
Identify Invalid Conditions
Conditions involving unknown realities, like exact pregnancy or milk quantity, are corrupt (shart fasid) and invalidate the sale.
Because they are not verifiable at the time.
4
Apply to New Cases
Given a scenario, determine if the stipulation is a valid description or a corrupt condition based on gharar.
Ask: Is the trait known and certain, or does it depend on an unknown outcome?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار الشرط
أخذه بكل الثمن أو ترك الوصف المرغوب فيه
أخذه إلخ [ أي أخذ المشتري العبد بكل المسمى إن شاء (عيني)] لأن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن؛
رجع
لكونها تابعة، ولو امتنع الرد بسبب من الأسباب، والمسألة بحالها، بالنقصان بأن يقوم كاتباً وغير كاتب، فيرجع بالتفاوت وليس كل الأوصاف يصح العقد باشتراطها، بل الضابط فيها أن كل وصف لا غرر فيه فاشتراطه جائز، لا ما فيه غرر، إلا أن يكون اشتراطه بمعنى البراءة من وجوده بأن لا يكون مرغوباً فيه. وعلى هذا لو باع ناقة أو شاة على أنها حامل، أو تحلب كذا وكذا أرطالاً، حيث يفسد البيع؛ لأنه ليس من قبيل الوصف، وإنما هو من قبيل الشرط الفاسد؛ إذ لا يعرف الحمل واللبن حقيقة؛ لأنه يحتمل أنه لبن أو دم أو حمل أو انتفاخ، حتى لو شرط أنها حلوب أو لبون لا يفسد؛ لأنه وصف ولو قال: إن العبد يخبز كذا صاعاً، أو يكتب كذا قدراً يفسد؛ لأن الوصف لا تعلق له في المقدار، فلا يكون وصفاً بل شرطا فاسداً، والجارية كالعبد في جميع الأحكام. (عيني، فتح)
Chapter of Option of Condition: He takes it with the full price or leaves the desired description. 'He takes it etc.' meaning the buyer takes the slave with the full price if he wishes, because descriptions are not compensated by any part of the price; he returns it due to their being subsidiary. And if returning is prevented by some cause while the case remains as it is, then [he takes] compensation for the defect by appraising it as a writer and non-writer, and he takes the difference. Not every description makes the contract valid when stipulated; rather the rule is that every description without uncertainty (gharar) is valid to stipulate, not one with uncertainty, unless the stipulation is in the sense of being free from its existence, i.e., that it is not desired. Based on this, if one sells a she-camel or goat on condition that it is pregnant, or that it milks such-and-such pounds, then the sale is invalid because it is not a description but a corrupt condition; for pregnancy and milk are not known in reality, as it could be milk or blood or pregnancy or swelling. However, if he stipulates that it is a milker or lactating, it does not invalidate because it is a description. And if he says: 'The slave bakes such-and-such sa's, or writes such-and-such amount,' it invalidates because a description does not relate to quantity, so it is not a description but a corrupt condition. The female slave is like the male slave in all rulings.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, a Hanafi legal text, on the chapter of Khiyar al-Shart. It addresses the effect of stipulating descriptions in a sale contract. The key principle is that descriptions (awṣāf) which are free from uncertainty (gharar) can be validly stipulated; if they involve uncertainty, the stipulation is corrupt and invalidates the sale. The text explains that if a buyer takes the sold item with a description lacking (e.g., a slave who is not a writer as claimed), he may either take it with full price or return it. But if return is impossible, he gets compensation for the defect. The invalid descriptions are those whose reality is unknown, like pregnancy or exact milk quantity, because these could be something else. Simple descriptions like 'milker' are valid because they are known. Quantitative descriptions of skill (baking a specific amount, writing a specific amount) are invalid because quantity is not a description. This applies equally to male and female slaves. The commentary from 'Ayni and Fath supports this.

Key Terms
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract; here referring to descriptions whose reality is unknown, such as exact pregnancy or milk quantity.
وصف (wasf): A description of the sold item that is known and free from uncertainty, e.g., 'writer' or 'milker'.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A condition that is corrupt, making the contract fasid; here involves unknown quantities or realities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on selling a goat with the condition that it milks exactly 5 pounds daily?
A) The sale is valid because the condition is a description.
B) The sale is invalid because the condition involves uncertainty (gharar).
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer inspects the goat first.
D) The sale is invalid unless the price is reduced.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a valid description is one without gharar; any condition involving unknown quantity or reality invalidates the contract.

Khiyar al-Ru'ya: The Option of Inspection for Unseen Purchases
Sharh of Khiyar al-Ru'ya in Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buying an unseen item, identify the buyer's option, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i new view.

Core Idea
Buying an unseen item is valid; the buyer may return it upon sight due to incomplete consent.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection). The Hanafi school holds that a sale of an unseen item is valid, but the buyer has the right to return it when seen. This option arises from a defect in consent, not from a defect in the item itself. We will explore the ruling, its evidence, and the disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Buying something unseen. What is the hukm? Ja'iz (valid) with khiyar for the buyer.
The seller has no option in a price sale (final Hanafi view).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith from al-Daraqutni: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it.'
This is a specific, albeit ahad, hadith.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The phrase 'falahu al-khiyar' indicates a legal right; the imperative implies permissibility and establishes the option.
The hadith is zanni in both thubut and dalala, but sufficient for this ruling.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Which school disagrees? Imam al-Shafi'i in his new view invalidates the sale due to jahl (ignorance). His proof: 'Do not sell what is not with you.'
The Shafi'i old view agrees with the Hanafis.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
How does the Hanafi school respond? The prohibition is interpreted as selling what one does not own, not what is unseen. Jahl here is tolerated because the buyer can return the item. The Hanafi hadith is specific and takes precedence.
This is the mufta bihi position.

شراء ما لم يره جائز، وله أن يرده إذا رآه وإن رضي قبله؛ ولا خيار لمن باع
كزيت في زقى أو برا في حوالق
المشتري
باب خيار الرؤية أي خيار عدم الرؤية، فحينئذ الإضافة من إضافة المسبب إلى السبب؛ إذ عدم الرؤية سبب لثبوت الخيار، ويحتمل أن يكون الإضافة من إضافة الشيء إلى شرطه؛ لأن هذا الخيار يثبت بشرط الرؤية، ويثبت هذا الخيار في كل عين ملكت بعقد يحتمل الفسخ كالشراء، فلا يثبت في المسلم فيه ولا في الأثمان الخالصة لثبوت كل في الذمة، ولا في المهر وبدل الخلع والصلح عن القصاص؛ لعدم قبولها الفسخ وينبغي أن يكون كذلك بدل العتق والكتابة، وقد نظم العلامة الحموي ما يثبت فيه خيار الرؤية، فقال:
الصفة،
في أربع خيار رؤية يرى إجارة وقسمة كذا الشراء
كذاك صلح في ادعاء المال فاحفظ سريعاً نظمها في الحال
ثم اعلم أن خيار الرؤية يمنع تمام الحكم؛ لخلل في الرضا، ولهذا كان له رده قبل الرؤية، ولو تصرف فيه جاز تصرفه وبطل خياره، وخيار الشرط يمنع نفس الحكم، فكان أقوى في المانعية، ثم خيار العيب يمنع لزوم الحكم، فكان أضعف من الكل، ولهذا قدم المصنف الأقوى، ثم ذكر خيار الرؤية، ثم خيار العيب. (فتح) ما لم يره المراد بالرؤية العلم بالمقصود، فتكون الحقيقة فردا من أفراد المجاز؛ لأن من المبيع ما لم يعلم منه المقصود بالرؤية بل بالشم مثلا في نحو المسك. رمز الحقائق: ۱۷/۲] جائز عندنا، إذا اتفقا على أنه موجود في ملكه. (ط)] لقوله عليها: "من اشترى شيئاً لم يره فله الخيار إذا رآه، رواه الدار قطني، وظاهر إطلاقه يقتضي عدم اشتراط الإشارة إليه، وعند مالك وأحمد: يصح بيعه ببيان الصفة، ويثبت للمشتري الخيار إذا رآه ولم يكن المبيع بهذه وهو قول الشافعي في القديم. رمز الحقائق: ١٧/٢] وفي القول الجديد لا يجوز لجهالة المبيع، وقد نهى عليها عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان، أي ما ليس بحاضر عند المتبايعين، وروى البخاري وأبو داود عن حكيم بن حزام الله قال: يا رسول الله ! يأتيني الرجل فيسألني عن بيع ما ليس عندي، فأبيع منه ثم أبتاعه من السوق فأسلم له؟ فقال له: "لا تبع ما ليس عندك ، ولنا ما روينا؛ ولأن الجهالة فيه لا تفضي إلى المنازعة؛ لأنه إذا لم يوافقه رده فصار كجهالة الوصف في الشاهد المعاين، والمراد بالنهي عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان ما ليس في ملكه؛ لأن حكيما كان يبيع ما لا يملكه، ثم يدخل في السوق فيشتريه ويسلم. (عيني، فتح) أن يرده: أي يرد الشيء الذي اشتراه و لم يره؛ لقوله علي من اشترى شيئاً لم يره فله الخيار إذا رآه". (عيني) وإن رضي واصل بما قبله يعني له الخيار إذا رآه ولو رضي قبل أن يراه بأن قال: رضيت به. (عيني) ولا خيار إلخ مراده إذا باع بالثمن، فلو كان بيع سلعة بسلعة فلكل منهما الخيار وكان أبو حنيفة يقول أولا: للبائع الخيار ثم رجع وقال: لا خيار له؛ لأن عثمان بن عفان الله باع أرضاً بالبصرة من طلحة . بن عبيد الله نه
Chapter of Option of Inspection: i.e., explaining its rulings. Buying what one has not seen is permissible, and he has the right to return it when he sees it, even if he was pleased before seeing. And there is no option for the seller... like oil in a skin or wheat in sacks. The buyer. Chapter of Option of Inspection meaning the option of non-sight; then the addition is of the effect to the cause, since non-sight is the cause for establishing the option. It may also be addition of the thing to its condition, because this option is established by the condition of sight. This option is established in every specific item owned by a contract that can be rescinded, like purchase. So it is not established in salam (advance payment) nor in pure prices because each is established in the dhimmah, nor in dower, nor in the compensation for khul', nor in compromise for qisas because they do not admit rescission. Similarly for the compensation for emancipation and kitabah. The scholar al-Hamawi composed a poem on where khiyar al-ru'ya applies: 'In four, option of sight is seen: ijarah, qismah, likewise purchase; likewise sulh in a claim of property, so memorize quickly its composition now.' Then know that khiyar al-ru'ya prevents the completion of the ruling due to a defect in consent; hence he may return it before sight. If he disposes of it, his disposition is valid and his option is void. Khiyar al-shart prevents the ruling itself, so it is stronger in prevention. Then khiyar al-'ayb prevents the binding of the ruling, so it is the weakest. Therefore the author presented the strongest first, then khiyar al-ru'ya, then khiyar al-'ayb. (Fath) 'What he has not seen': what is meant by sight is knowledge of the intended purpose; so the literal sight is one instance of a broader meaning, because some sold items are not known by sight but by smell, like musk. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 17/2) It is permissible according to us, if they agree that it exists in his possession. (T) Due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it' – narrated by al-Daraqutni. Its apparent generality requires that pointing to it is not a condition. According to Malik and Ahmad: its sale is valid with a description, and the buyer has the option when he sees it if the sold item is not as described; this is also the old view of al-Shafi'i. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 17/2) In the new view, it is not permissible due to ignorance of the sold item. And he (peace be upon him) forbade selling what is not with a person, i.e., not present with the two transacting parties. Al-Bukhari and Abu Dawud narrated from Hakim ibn Hizam (may Allah be pleased with him): 'O Messenger of Allah, a man comes to me asking me to sell what is not with me; so I sell it to him then I buy it from the market and deliver it?' He said: 'Do not sell what is not with you.' Our proof is what we have narrated; and because the ignorance in this does not lead to dispute, since if it does not match him, he returns it, so it becomes like ignorance of description in a present item seen. And the meaning of the prohibition of selling what is not with a person is what is not in his possession, because Hakim used to sell what he did not own, then enter the market and buy it and deliver. ('Ayni, Fath) 'That he returns it': i.e., returns the thing he bought and did not see, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it.' ('Ayni) 'Even if he was pleased before seeing': meaning he has the option when he sees it even if he was pleased before seeing, by saying 'I am pleased with it.' ('Ayni) 'And there is no option...' etc. His meaning is when he sells for a price; if it is a barter, then each has the option. Abu Hanifa initially said the seller has the option, then he retracted and said: He has no option, because 'Uthman ibn 'Affan (may Allah be pleased with him) sold land in Basra to Talha ibn 'Ubaydillah...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the chapter of Khiyar al-Ru'ya (option of inspection) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by defining the term: the option arises from not having seen the item at purchase. The ruling is that buying an unseen item is valid (permissible) in the Hanafi school, and the buyer has the right to return it upon seeing it, even if he previously expressed satisfaction. The seller has no such option when selling for a price, but in barter both parties have the option (according to Abu Hanifa's final ruling). The text then specifies where this option applies: in contracts that can be rescinded, like purchase, ijarah, qismah, and sulh on property. It does not apply in salam, pure currencies, dower, khul' compensation, or qisas compromise because those do not admit rescission. The passage then compares khiyar al-ru'ya with khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-'ayb, ranking them in strength. The proof is the hadith from al-Daraqutni: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it.' The text also addresses the Shafi'i disagreement: the old view agrees with the Hanafis, but the new view requires the item to be present. The Hanafis argue that the ignorance here does not lead to dispute because the buyer can return it. The prohibition on selling what is not with you is interpreted as selling what one does not own, not merely unseen items. The passage ends with an anecdote about 'Uthman selling land to Talha.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of the buyer to return an item purchased without sight after seeing it, due to lack of full consent.
جهالة (jahl): Ignorance about the specific item; here it refers to not seeing the item before purchase.
تمام الحكم (tamam al-hukm): The completion of the legal effect of the contract; khiyar al-ru'ya prevents this due to a defect in consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, which of the following contracts does NOT establish khiyar al-ru'ya for the buyer?
A) Buying a house unseen.
B) Salam (advance payment) for a described item.
C) Ijarah (lease) of an unseen property.
D) Barter exchange of two unseen items.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, buying an unseen item is valid but the buyer retains the right to return it upon first sight, due to incomplete consent.

The Option of Inspection: Proof, Nature, and Nullifiers
Ruling and evidence for the option of inspection and its nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of an unseen item, explain the evidence from ijma, and apply the rules for nullification of the option of inspection.

Core Idea
The right to rescind a sale of an unseen item is established indefinitely by the consensus of the Companions.

In Hanafi fiqh, buying or selling an item you have not seen is valid but comes with an option to rescind upon sight. This lesson unpacks the proof for this ruling from the incident of Talha and Uthman, the nature of the option as unlimited, and the specific acts that nullify it—critical for any real estate or e-commerce transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
The matn states: 'What he has not seen, and it is voided by what voids the option of stipulation.' The ruling is that a sale of an unseen item is valid but carries the option of inspection. First, identify the act (sale of unseen item) and the legal effect (option to rescind).
The option applies to both buyer and seller, as shown by the incident where both Talha (buyer) and Uthman (seller) claimed the option.
2
Locate the Proof and Understand Its Strength
The passage cites the incident of Talha and Uthman referring to Jubayr ibn Mut'im, who ruled in favor of Talha's option, and that this occurred in the presence of other Companions without objection. This forms a consensus (ijma). Classify the evidence: type = ijma, thubut = zanni (since transmission of the incident is ahad), dalala = qati (the Companions' agreement is definitive for the rule).
The Hanafi school considers ijma of the Companions as a binding proof, even if the narrated incident is ahad.
3
Note the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that selling an unseen item is absolutely void (batil) due to gharar. The Hanafi response is that the incident from the Companions provides a specific exception, and the option itself removes the uncertainty. Identify the practical consequence: in Hanafi, the contract is enforceable with an option; in Shafi'i, the contract is null.
This is a genuine ikhtilaf; both schools have evidence for their positions.
4
Understand the Nature of the Option: No Time Limit
The commentary (Fath) states that the option of inspection is established absolutely, not limited to a duration, and this is the most correct position (al-ashah). The buyer may rescind at any time until he expressly or implicitly indicates satisfaction. Contrast with khiyar al-shart, which is time-limited.
The option is indefinite; it does not expire on its own.
5
Identify the Nullifiers and Distinguish from Khiyar al-Shart
The passage lists acts that void the option: explicit satisfaction, pointing out a defect, irreversible physical acts (e.g., intercourse, kissing with desire), and acts creating rights for others (e.g., sale, gift with delivery, pledge, lease). Importantly, acts that do not create a right for another (e.g., bargaining, sale with option for seller, gift without delivery) do NOT void it. Also, for khiyar al-ru'ya, these nullifiers only take effect after sight; prior to sight, even explicit satisfaction does not void the option. For khiyar al-shart, nullifiers can void the option even before sight.
The key difference: khiyar al-ru'ya is suspended until actual inspection; khiyar al-shart is active immediately but time-limited.

كتاب البيوع باب خيار الرؤية ما لم يره، ويبطل بما يبطل به خيار الشرط، خيار الرؤية وهي اسم لكوم من الحب الصحابة فقيل لطلحة إنك غبنت فقال لي الخيار؛ لأني اشتريت ما لم أره، وقيل لعثمان الله : إنك قد غبنت، فقال: لي الخيار؛ لأني بعت ما لم أره، فحكما جبير بن مطعم له، فقضى بالخيار لطلحة، وكان ذلك بمحضر من فيكون إجماعاً، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز بيع ما لم يره أصلاً قولاً واحداً. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۸/۲] (فتح) ما لم يره بأن ورث شيئاً فباعه قبل الرؤية. (ط) ويبطل فيه إيماء إلى أن خيار الرؤية يثبت مطلقاً غير مؤقت بمدة، وهو الأصح، فيكون له الفسخ في جميع عمره ما لم يسقطه بقول أو فعل يدل على الرضاء به؛ لإطلاق النص. (فتح) بما يبطل به إلخ كالتصريح بالرضاء أو التعييب أو تصرف لا يحل كالوطء والقبلة واللمس بشهوة، أو لا يرفع كالإعتاق والتدبير، أو حقاً يوجب للغير كالبيع، ولو بشرط الخيار للمشتري، والهبة مع التسليم، والرهن والإجارة إلا أنه في هذه لا يتقيد بما بعد ثبوته بل يبطل، قبله ومعناه: خروجه عن صلاحية أن يثبت له الخيار عندها، وإلا فالخيار معلق بها، فكيف يبطل قبلها، بخلاف ما لا يوجب حقاً للغير كالبيع بخيار للبائع، والمساومة، والهبة بلا تسليم وبهذا اندفع إيراد الأخذ بالشفعة، والعرض على البيع، فإن خيار الشرط يبطل بهما دون الرؤية؛ لأن الرضاء صريحاً غير مسقط له قبله، فالدلالة أولى. (فتح) خيار الشرط عن التصريح والدلالة ومراده بعد الرؤية، وأما قبلها، فلا يسقط. (عيني)
The Book of Sales, Chapter of the Option of Inspection. What he has not seen, and it is voided by what voids the option of stipulation. The option of inspection: it is a name for a heap of grain. So it was said to Talha: 'You have been deceived.' He said: 'I have the option because I bought what I had not seen.' And it was said to Uthman: 'You have been deceived.' He said: 'I have the option because I sold what I had not seen.' They both submitted to Jubayr ibn Mut'im for judgment, and he ruled in favor of Talha's option, and that was in the presence of the Companions, thus it became consensus. And al-Shafi'i said: Selling what one has not seen is absolutely impermissible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 18/2] (Fath) 'What he has not seen' means if he inherited something and sold it before seeing it. (T) 'And it is voided' alludes to the fact that the option of inspection is established absolutely, not limited to a duration, and this is the most correct position. So he has the right to rescind for his entire lifetime as long as he does not cancel it by a statement or action indicating satisfaction, due to the unrestricted text. (Fath) 'By what voids it' includes explicit satisfaction, or pointing out a defect, or an action that cannot be reversed like sexual intercourse, kissing, or touching with desire, or an action that does not reverse like manumission or tadbir, or a right that obligates to another like sale, even if with a condition of option for the buyer, or a gift with delivery, or pledge, or lease, except that in these it is not restricted to after its establishment but rather it is voided before it, meaning the item becomes ineligible for the option to be established for it at that point, otherwise the option is suspended by them, so how can it be voided before them? Unlike that which does not create a right for another, like a sale with an option for the seller, or bargaining, or a gift without delivery. By this the objection of taking by preemption and presenting for sale is repelled, for the option of stipulation is voided by these two but not the option of inspection, because explicit satisfaction does not cancel it before sight, so indication is even more so. (Fath) The option of stipulation is by explicit statement or indication, and its intent is after sight, but before it, it is not cancelled. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on the Option of Inspection (Khayar al-Ru'ya). It begins by stating the matn: 'What he has not seen, and it is voided by what voids the option of stipulation.' The commentary then provides the foundational evidence for the option: the well-known incident where Talha and Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them) both claimed the option after being deceived in transactions involving unseen items. They referred to Jubayr ibn Mut'im, who ruled in favor of Talha's right to rescind, and this was witnessed by the Companions, forming a consensus (ijma). Against this, Imam al-Shafi'i held that selling an unseen item is absolutely invalid. The Hanafi position is that the option is established indefinitely—not limited to a specific period—and the buyer may rescind at any time unless he explicitly or implicitly indicates satisfaction. The commentary then details what nullifies the option: explicit words of satisfaction, pointing out a defect, irreversible physical acts (like intercourse), and acts that create rights for others (like sale or gift with delivery). Importantly, acts that merely indicate satisfaction but do not create a permanent right (like bargaining) do not nullify the option before sight. The distinction between khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart is highlighted: the latter can be voided by implicit indication even before sight, whereas the former cannot.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khayār al-ru’yah): The right to rescind a sale upon seeing the item, established when the item was unseen at the time of contract.
خيار الشرط (khayār al-shart): The option of stipulation, where the parties agree to a specified period to decide.
إجماع (ijmā‘): Consensus of the Companions, used as evidence for the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a buyer explicitly saying 'I am satisfied' before seeing the item?
A) The option of inspection is voided immediately.
B) The option of inspection is not voided because it is only effective after sight.
C) The option of inspection becomes time-limited to three days.
D) The sale becomes void.

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) is an indefinite right established by ijma, allowing the buyer to rescind a sale of an unseen item until satisfaction is expressed, and it is nullified by explicit or implicit indications of acceptance.

Sufficiency of Sight: Face and Hindquarters for Beasts
Ruling on which parts suffice to remove the option of inspection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirements for seeing a purchased item before sale to remove the option of inspection and distinguish the rulings for slaves, beasts, and edibles.

Core Idea
For a riding beast, seeing only the face does not remove the option; the hindquarters must also be seen.

When a buyer purchases an item without seeing it, Islamic law grants an option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah). The pivotal question is: what minimal view of the item suffices to waive this option? This lesson examines the Hanafi position, focusing on the distinction between homogeneous piles, slaves, riding beasts, and other animals, as well as an intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize that the passage deals with khiyar al-ru'yah — the buyer's right to rescind a sale after seeing an unseen item.
The option is removed only if the buyer saw the item before purchase, not after.
2
Categorize the purchased item
Determine whether the item is a homogeneous pile (sabra), a slave, a riding beast (horse, donkey, mule), or another animal (sheep, cow, camel).
Different rules apply to each category.
3
Apply the minimal sight requirement
For a pile, seeing any part suffices. For a slave, seeing most of the face suffices. For a riding beast, seeing both face and hindquarters is required (mufta bihi).
For food items, tasting is required.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that seeing the face alone suffices for a beast, analogizing to a slave. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) require the hindquarters because the posterior is a significant part.
The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs.
5
Note exceptions and conditions
For animals kept for milk or offspring, more extensive inspection (e.g., udder, entire body) is required. For certain beasts, Abu Hanifa also required seeing hooves and tail.
These are additional rulings from the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كفت رؤية :إلخ : معناه أنه لو رأى ما ذكر ثم اشترى فلا خيار له وليس المراد أنه بعد الشراء يسقط خياره برؤية وجه الصبرة كما توهمه بعض الطلبة، واستشكله بأنه غير مؤقت على الأصح بل له الفسخ في جميع عمره ما لم يوجد مبطله وقوله : ووجه الرقيق، يكتفي برؤية أكثر الوجه، وقوله : ووجه الدابة" المراد بالدابة الفرس والحمار والبغل، والتقييد بالفرس ونحوه للاحتراز عما لو كانت شاة للحم أو للدر والنسل أو كانت بقرة أو ناقة للحلب، فالمراد بالدابة التي تركب فإن كانت شاة لحم فلابد من حبسها، وإن كانت شاة قنية احترازاً عن الشاة للدر والنسل فلابد من النظر إلى ضرعها وسائر جسدها. وشرط بعضهم رؤية القوائم مع الوجه والكفل، والصحيح عدم الاشتراط، وفي ما يطعم لابد من الذوق، وعن أبي حنيفة أن في البرذون والبغل والحمار يشترط رؤية الحافر والذنب أيضاً، وإنما ذكر الكفل في الدابة؛ لأنه إذا رأى وجهها فقط لا يسقط خياره؛ لأن المؤخر موضع مقصود فيها، بخلاف الرقيق، وهو قول الشيخين، وقال محمد: لا خيار له؛ لأن الأصل في الحيوان الوجه، فيكتفى برؤيته كالعبد. رمز الحقائق: ۱۸/۲] (فتح) والرقيق: أي وكفت رؤية وجه الرقيق رجلا أو امرأة. (ط، عيني) و كفلها بفتحتين ما يكون من جانب الذنب، يقال له بالفارسية: فرخج. (معدن)
And the sufficiency of seeing: its meaning is that if he saw what was mentioned then purchased, he has no option. It is not meant that after purchase his option falls by seeing the face of the pile, as some students misunderstood, and they objected that it is not time-limited according to the most correct view, rather he has rescission for his entire lifetime as long as no voiding factor exists. And his statement 'and the face of the slave' — seeing most of the face suffices. And his statement 'and the face of the beast' — the meaning of beast is horse, donkey, and mule. The restriction to a horse and the like is to exclude if it were a sheep for meat or for milk and offspring, or if it were a cow or camel for milking. So the intended meaning is the beast that is ridden. If it were a sheep for meat, then it must be confined. And if it were a sheep for breeding, as opposed to for milk and offspring, then one must look at its udder and entire body. Some scholars required seeing the legs along with the face and hindquarters, but the correct view is that it is not required. And for edibles, tasting is necessary. And from Abu Hanifa: for the bardhoun, mule, and donkey, seeing the hoof and tail is also required. And the mention of the hindquarters in the beast is because if he sees only its face, his option does not fall, because the posterior is a significant part of it, unlike the slave. This is the view of the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad said: he has no option because the essential in an animal is the face, so seeing it suffices as with a slave. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 18/2] (Fath) 'And the slave' means seeing the face of a male or female slave suffices. (T, Ayni) 'And its hindquarters' with two fatha is what is by the side of the tail, called in Persian 'farkhaj'. (Ma'din)
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage explains the matn ruling that seeing the face of a pile of grain, a slave, or a riding animal plus its hindquarters suffices to remove the option of inspection. The commentary first corrects a misunderstanding: the option is not dropped by merely seeing the face after purchase; rather, if one sees the item and then buys it, there is no option. The option is indefinite. Then it details: seeing most of the face of a slave suffices. For beasts of burden (horse, donkey, mule), just the face is not enough; the hindquarters must also be seen because the rump is a significant part. However, animals kept for other purposes (e.g., sheep for meat or milk) require more extensive inspection—the udder and entire body for milk animals, or confinement for meat animals. There is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs) hold that seeing only the face of a beast does not remove the option, while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that seeing the face is sufficient, analogizing to a slave. The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs. Additionally, for food items, tasting is required. For a bardhoun (a type of horse), mule, or donkey, Abu Hanifa also requires seeing the hoof and tail. The passage ends with a linguistic note on 'kifl' (hindquarters).

Key Terms
الصبرة (al-ṣabra): A heap or pile of grain or similar commodity.
الرقيق (al-raqīq): A slave, male or female.
الدابة (al-dābba): A riding beast, specifically horse, donkey, mule, and similar.
الكفل (al-kifl): The hindquarters or rump of an animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when purchasing a horse that you have not seen, what must you see to remove the option of inspection?
A) The face only.
B) The face and the hindquarters.
C) The face, hindquarters, and hooves.
D) The entire body.

Key Takeaway
For a riding beast, seeing both face and hindquarters before purchase is required to remove the option of inspection; the face alone is insufficient.

Sufficiency of Partial Sight in Sales: Garments and Houses
Ruling on sufficiency of partial sight for khiyar al-ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on sufficiency of partial sight for khiyar al-ru'ya, distinguish between uniform and non-uniform items, and apply the mufta bihi position in contemporary contexts.

Core Idea
Partial sight suffices only for uniform items; custom can change rulings.

The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) allows the buyer to invalidate a contract if the item seen later differs from what was shown. This passage asks: does seeing only a part suffice to remove that option? The answer depends on whether the item is uniform. Learn how the Hanafi school handles folded garments and house interiors, and why today's fatwa adopts a stricter view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage rules on khiyar al-ru'ya: seeing the exterior of a folded garment or the courtyard of a house is sufficient to remove the option, but only if the item is uniform (parts do not differ significantly). If it is not uniform, full inspection is required.
Uniformity is key.
2
Locate the Dalil
The dalil here is talil (rationale): because for a uniform cloth, seeing one part indicates the rest, as ends are nearly identical. For a house, seeing the courtyard was sufficient based on Kufan custom where rooms were alike.
No direct textual proof (Quran/Hadith); it is based on reason and custom.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The legal linkage: the condition for sufficiency is uniformity (the item is 'مما لا يختلف' or 'ذوات الأمثال'). If the parts are uniform, partial sight provides knowledge of the whole. If not, partial sight does not suffice.
Note the distinction: uniform = ذوات الأمثال; non-uniform = ليس من ذوات الأمثال.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar requires full inspection in all cases, even for a uniform garment, because he argues that the whole cannot be known by part. The majority (jamhur) allow partial sight for uniform items.
Zufar's view is stricter.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The passage states 'الفتوى على قوله' for the garment (fatwa on Zufar) and 'وهو الأصح' for the house (Zufar's view is more correct). The reasoning: custom has changed – houses are no longer uniform, so full inspection is necessary. Thus, the mufta bihi is Zufar's view for both.
Custom ('urf) changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهر الثوب: أي وكفت رؤية ظاهر الثوب حال كونه مطوياً؛ لأنه يستدل برؤية طرف منه على الباقي؛ إذ لا تتفاوت أطراف الثوب الواحد إلا يسيرا وذا غير معتبر، قال الأقطع ومتى وجد داخل الثوب مختلفاً فهو عيب، ولا تعلق له بخيار الرؤية، وعند زفر: لابد من نشره ورؤية كله، والفتوى على قوله، وفي "النهر" [٣٨٣/٣] "المبسوط " : : الجواب على ما قاله ،زفر وعند الجمهور: لا حاجة إلى النشر إذا لم يكن في طي الثوب ما يكون مقصوداً، فإن كان في باطن الثوب ما يقصد بالنظر كموضع العلم، ووجه العمامة ونحوه، فحينئذ لا يسقط خياره بدون النشر عند الكل. (عيني، فتح) عن جس لحم شاة وداخل الدار: التعسر رؤية الجميع، وكفي ونظر ضرع شاة وذوق مطعوم وشم مشموم (عيني، ط) ] أي كفت رؤية داخل الدار فإنها كرؤية كلها، وفي عامة الروايات: إذا رأى صحن الدار يسقط خياره وإن لم ير بيوتها، وكذا إذا رأى خارج الدار أو أشجار البستان من خارج وقال :زفر لا بد من رؤية داخل البيوت وهو الأصح؛ لأن منافع البيوت الداخلة تختلف فلابد من رؤية ذلك كله؛ لأنه ليس من ذوات الأمثال، فلا يعرف کله برؤية بعضه، وما ذكره الشيخ مبني على عادة أهل الكوفة، فإن في ذلك الزمان دورهم كانت على تقطيع واحد، وأما اليوم فبخلافه فلا يكتفى برؤية داخل بالدار. [ رمز الحقائق: ١٩/٢] (فتح، مسكين)
And the outside of the garment: meaning seeing the exterior of the garment while it is folded suffices, because seeing one part indicates the rest, since the parts of a single piece of cloth do not differ except slightly, and that is negligible. Al-Aqta' said: if the inside of the cloth is found to be different, it is a defect, and it is not related to the option of inspection. According to Zufar, it is necessary to unfold it and see all of it, and the fatwa is on his statement. In al-Nahr [3/383] and al-Mabsut: the answer is what Zufar said. According to the majority, there is no need to unfold if there is nothing in the fold that is intended. If there is something in the inner part of the cloth that is meant to be seen, such as the place of the emblem or the face of the turban and the like, then his option does not fall without unfolding, according to all. (Ayni, Fath) Regarding touching the flesh of sheep and the interior of the house: seeing all is difficult, and it suffices, and seeing the udder of a sheep, tasting food, and smelling fragrant items (Ayni, T) — meaning seeing the interior of the house suffices as if seeing all of it. In most transmissions, if he sees the courtyard of the house, his option falls, even if he does not see its rooms. Similarly, if he sees the outside of the house or the trees of the garden from outside. Zufar said: seeing the inside of the rooms is necessary, and that is the most correct, because the benefits of the interior rooms differ, so it is necessary to see all of that, because it is not of uniform nature, so the whole cannot be known by seeing a part. What the shaykh mentioned is based on the custom of the people of Kufa, for in that time their houses were of uniform layout, but today it is different, so seeing the interior of the house does not suffice. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 19/2] (Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage covers two separate but related rulings: (1) seeing the exterior of a folded garment, and (2) seeing the interior of a house (or courtyard). For a garment, if it is a uniform cloth (like a simple fabric), seeing the outside while folded is sufficient because the ends are similar. However, if the cloth has an important design on the inside (e.g., an emblem or the face of a turban), then the garment must be opened to see that part. Imam Zufar required unfolding in all cases, and the fatwa is on his view (according to some sources). The Hanafi school reconciles: if there is nothing special in the fold, seeing the outside suffices; otherwise, it does not. For a house, the rule is that seeing the courtyard (sahn) is considered like seeing the entire house, assuming rooms are similar in layout. However, this ruling was based on the custom of Kufa where houses were built uniformly. Today, with varied house designs, simply seeing the courtyard does not remove the option; one must see all rooms whose benefits differ. Zufar's stricter view is considered the more correct (al-ashah) for modern times. Thus, the reliance on custom ('urf) is evident: rulings can change with changing customs.

Key Terms
الثوب (al-thawb): Garment or cloth, specifically a piece of fabric.
الصحن (al-sahn): The courtyard or open area of a house.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom, which influences fiqh rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school today, when buying a house without seeing the interior rooms, what is the ruling?
A) Seeing the courtyard suffices to remove the option.
B) One must see every room because their benefits differ.
C) The option is removed only if the house is in Kufa.
D) The house must be fully inspected including the roof.

Key Takeaway
Partial sight suffices for uniform items; non-uniform items or changed customs require full inspection.

Agent vs Messenger: Effect of Their Sight on the Option
Ruling on sight of agent vs messenger in option of inspection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between a wakil and a rasul in Hanafi sales law, and apply the ruling on whether their sight removes the buyer's option of inspection, including the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
An agent's sight removes the option; a messenger's does not—but the relied-upon view says neither removes it.

In Islamic sales law, the buyer has an option to return an unseen item upon inspection. But what if an agent or messenger receives the item on his behalf? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explained by al-'Ayni, distinguishes the two roles and clarifies the effect of their sight. The key is the legal capacity of the agent versus the mere conveyor role of the messenger.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two roles
Identify the defining feature of a wakil (agent): he has authority to litigate and act independently. A rasul (messenger) is only a conveyor; he cannot sue or take legal action.
This distinction is the basis for the different rulings.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, the sight of a wakil at the time of receipt drops the buyer's option (unless a defect is discovered later). The sight of a rasul does not drop it.
The ruling is based on the agent's full representation.
3
Introduce the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) disagree: they hold that neither the wakil's nor the rasul's sight drops the option. Both leave the buyer's right to rescind intact.
Their reasoning: the buyer personally must see the item.
4
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The passage indicates that the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the view of the Sahibayn. This is because it is the opinion of 'the three' (Abu Hanifa's other students) and is stronger in transmission.
Thus the buyer may return the item even if the agent saw it.
5
Identify the temporal condition and the exception for purchase agents
Only sight at the time of receipt matters; prior or subsequent sight has no effect. Additionally, the dispute applies only to agents for receipt; for an agent appointed to purchase, his sight drops the option by consensus.
This shows the ruling is specific to the type of agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيله صورته: أن يقول المشتري لغيره كن وكيلاً عني بقبض المبيع. وصورة الرسول: أن يقول: كن رسولاً عني بقبضه. والفرق بين الوكيل والرسول أن الوكيل يملك الخصومة إذا امتنع البائع عن التسليم. والرسول لا يملك، فلو طعاماً لم يره، فوكل رجلاً بالقبض، فقبضه الوكيل بعد ما رآه فليس للمشتري أن يرده إلا من عيب، والمعتبر رؤية التي مع القبض دون التي بعده، والرؤية قبل التوكيل لا أثر بها، فلا يسقط بها الخيار، وإن أرسل رسولاً بقبضه، فقبضه الرسول بعد ما رآه فللمشتري أن يرده وقالا الوكيل والرسول سواء، وللمشتري أن يرده إذا رآه، وهو رواية عنه، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وهذا الخلاف في الوكيل بالقبض، فأما الوكيل بالشراء فرؤيته تسقط الخيار إجماعاً. (عيني، فتح) كنظره: أي كنظر المشتري الموكل عند أبي حنيفة. (عيني) لا نظر رسوله: لأن الرسول لا يملك شيئاً، وإنما فوض إليه تبليغ الرسالة، ولهذا لا يملك القبض والتسليم إذا كان رسولاً في الشراء والبيع. (عيني، فتح)
His agent: its form is that the buyer says to another: 'Be an agent for me in receiving the sold item.' And the form of the messenger: that he says: 'Be a messenger for me to receive it.' The difference between the agent and the messenger is that the agent has the right to litigation if the seller refuses to deliver, while the messenger does not. So if [the buyer] purchased food that he had not seen, and he appointed a man as agent for receipt, and the agent received it after seeing it, then the buyer has no right to return it except due to a defect. The considered sight is the one at the time of receipt, not after it; sight before the agency has no effect, so the option is not dropped by it. If he sent a messenger to receive it, and the messenger received it after seeing it, then the buyer may return it. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: the agent and the messenger are the same, and the buyer may return it if the agent saw it. This is a narration from him [Abu Hanifa], and the three [imams] said that. This difference is regarding the agent for receipt; as for the agent for purchase, his sight drops the option by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) 'Like his sight' means like the sight of the principal buyer according to Abu Hanifa. (Ayni) 'Not the sight of his messenger' because the messenger possesses nothing; he is merely entrusted with conveying the message, and therefore he does not have the right of receipt and delivery if he is a messenger in purchase and sale. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between a wakil (agent) and a rasul (messenger) in the context of the option of inspection. The matn states that the sight of an agent for receipt is like the sight of the buyer himself, so it removes the option. The sight of a messenger does not remove the option. The commentary explains the forms: the agent is authorized to receive and has the right to litigate; the messenger is only a courier. According to Abu Hanifa, if the agent sees the item at the time of receipt, the buyer's option is dropped (unless there is a defect). However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule that the agent's sight does not drop the option, and the buyer can still return it. The relied-upon position here is that of the Sahibayn, as it is the view of the three imams (Abu Hanifa's other students). The difference applies only to an agent for receipt; for an agent for purchase, it is unanimous that his sight drops the option. Also, sight before the appointment of the agent has no effect; only sight at the time of receipt matters. The rationale: the messenger is merely a conveyor and does not act on behalf of the buyer in a legal capacity.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): An agent who has authority to act on behalf of the principal, including litigation.
الرسول (al-rasūl): A messenger who only conveys the message or item without legal authority.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Physical receipt or taking possession of the sold item.
الخصومة (al-khuṣūmah): Right to litigate or file a legal claim.
الخيار (al-khiyār): Option, specifically the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn), what happens if a buyer appoints a wakil (agent) to receive an unseen item, and the wakil sees it and takes receipt?
A) The buyer's option of inspection is dropped.
B) The buyer may still return the item upon seeing it.
C) The wakil's sight is considered like the buyer's sight.
D) The sale becomes void.

Key Takeaway
The sight of an agent for receipt does not drop the buyer's option of inspection in the relied-upon Hanafi view; only the buyer's personal sight at the time of contract or receipt terminates the option.

Legal Capacity of a Blind Person in Contracts and Religious Duties
Ruling on the validity of contracts by a blind person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a blind person's contract is valid, and enumerate the twelve matters where a blind person is treated differently from a sighted person.

Core Idea
A blind person's contract is fully valid, but he is exempt from duties requiring sight.

This lesson examines the legal capacity of a blind person in Islamic law. While a blind person is fully capable of entering into valid contracts (sale, purchase, etc.) because he is legally responsible and has needs, there are specific areas where his lack of sight exempts him or restricts his participation. The passage lists twelve such exceptions, balancing legal capacity with practical inability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text states 'صح عقده مطلقاً' – his contract is valid absolutely, whether sale or purchase.
This includes contracts on behalf of others.
2
Understand the rationale
The blind person is mukallaf and needs to buy food and clothing; thus he must be able to transact.
Legal capacity (ahliyyah) is preserved despite the disability.
3
List the twelve exceptions
The passage enumerates twelve matters where the blind person differs from a sighted person: no obligation of jihad, Friday prayer, congregation, Hajj (even with guide), cannot be a witness (even in hearsay), no diyah for his eye but only hukumat 'adl, adhan and imamah disliked unless most knowledgeable, cannot free slaves as expiation, cannot be supreme imam or judge, slaughter disliked.
These are limitations on capacity (ahliyyat al-ada).
4
Categorize the exceptions
Group the exceptions into: ritual worship (jihad, Hajj, prayer), leadership roles (witness, judge, imam, caliph), personal actions (slaughter, manumission), and torts (diyah for eye).
Sight is required for proper performance in these acts.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one saying) held that a blind person's purchase is invalid; the Hanafi school rejects this.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عقد الأعمى [بيعا أو شراء؛ لأنه مكلف محتاج إلى شراء المأكول والملبوس. (عيني)] ولو لغيره، وهو كالبصير إلا في اثني عشر مسألة: لا جهاد عليه، ولا جمعة ولا جماعة، ولا حج وإن وجده قائداً في الكل، ولا يصلح كونه شاهداً ولو فيما تقبل فيه الشهادة بالتسامع، ولا دية في عينه بل الواجب حكومة عدل، وكره أذانه وإمامته إلا أن يكون أعلم القوم، ولا يجوز إعتاقه عن الكفارات، ولا كونه إماماً أعظم ولا قاضياً، ويكره ذبحه، وصح عقده مطلقاً سواء كان بيعاً أو شراء، وعند الشافعي: لا يصح شراؤه في قول. (فتح عن البحر)
The contract of a blind person [for sale or purchase; because he is legally responsible and needs to buy food and clothing. (Ayni)] even on behalf of another, and he is like a sighted person except in twelve matters: jihad is not obligatory upon him, nor Friday prayer, nor congregational prayer, nor Hajj even if he finds a guide for all, and he is not suitable as a witness even in matters where testimony by hearsay is accepted, and there is no blood money for his eye but rather a just compensation (hukumat 'adl), and his adhan and imamah are disliked unless he is the most knowledgeable of the people, and it is not permissible for him to manumit slaves as expiation, nor to be a supreme imam nor a judge, and his slaughtering is disliked, and his contract is valid absolutely whether sale or purchase. According to al-Shafi'i, his purchase is not valid in one saying. (Fath from al-Bahr)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal capacity of a blind person (a'ma) in contracts. The matn states 'the contract of a blind person is valid' and the commentary elaborates. A blind person is legally responsible (mukallaf) and needs to engage in transactions like buying food and clothing. Therefore, his contracts are valid just like a sighted person, even if done on behalf of others. However, there are twelve exceptions where he is not treated equally: he is excused from jihad, Friday and congregational prayers, and Hajj (even with a guide); he cannot serve as a witness (even in cases of hearsay); his eye injury does not warrant full diyah but a discretionary compensation; his adhan (call to prayer) and leading prayer (imamah) are disliked unless he is the most knowledgeable; he cannot free slaves as expiation; he cannot be the supreme imam (caliph) or a judge; his slaughtering is disliked. Despite these, his contract is valid without exception. The Shafi'i school has a view that a blind person's purchase is invalid, but the Hanafi school rejects that. The exceptions are based on the inability to perform certain acts properly without sight.

Key Terms
الأعمى (al-a’mā): A blind person, i.e., someone who lacks eyesight.
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat ‘adl): A discretionary compensation assessed by a just judge, not a fixed amount.
اثني عشر مسألة (ithnā ‘ashara mas’ala): The twelve matters in which a blind person is treated differently from a sighted person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the twelve matters in which a blind person differs from a sighted person, according to the passage?
A) Jihad
B) Hajj
C) Selling property
D) Being a judge

Key Takeaway
A blind person's contract is valid like a sighted person's, but he is exempt from twelve specific duties and roles due to his lack of sight.

Pages p. 272 · Buyu — Sales and Trade
